_Thought for 1m 19s_

## Agent 3 — zero-prime anti-design attack

I am treating the assigned task as: determine whether same-scale zero source-flattening,

\[
\sum_j w_j\sin(k\theta_j)\approx 0,\qquad 3\le k\le R+3,
\]

forces an explicit-formula contradiction or whether it reduces to the same prime/AFE nonrecurrence theorem assigned to Agent 2. This follows the supplied Agent 3 mandate and shared Gate 1 background. 

## 1. Bottom-line diagnosis

The zero-prime route **does not currently give an independent contradiction**.

The same-scale zero anti-design condition can be pushed through the explicit formula, but the output is essentially:

\[
\boxed{
\text{source-flat zero packet}
\Rightarrow
\text{a finite vector of actual prime/AFE wavelet coefficients is very small}.
}
\]

Thus the route collapses to a structured version of the Agent 2 prime nonrecurrence problem.

The useful product of Agent 3 is therefore a sharper formulation of the prime obstruction: not a generic Dirichlet-polynomial small-ball statement, but a **packet-adapted spectral leakage theorem** for the actual zeta coefficients.

---

## 2. Normalized zero-side moment formulation

Let

\[
F_\lambda(x)=\lambda^3 q''_{\rm can}(m_0+\lambda x).
\]

In the same-scale local chart, the packet contribution has the model form

\[
F_\lambda(x)\approx \sum_j w_j K_{\theta_j}''(x),
\]

with

\[
K_\theta(x)=\frac{\sin\theta}{(x-\cos\theta)^2+\sin^2\theta},
\]

and

\[
K_\theta^{(n)}(0)=n!\sin((n+1)\theta).
\]

Therefore, for \(r\ge 0\),

\[
F_\lambda^{(r)}(0)
\sim
(r+2)!\sum_j w_j\sin((r+3)\theta_j)
+
\mathcal B_r,
\]

where \(\mathcal B_r\) denotes background, far-zero, gamma, tail, and canonicalization corrections.

So finite-jet source flattening is equivalent to

\[
\boxed{
\sum_j w_j\sin(k\theta_j)
=
-\mathcal B_{k-3}
+
O(\varepsilon),
\qquad 3\le k\le R+3.
}
\]

In the pure local model, \(\mathcal B_{k-3}=0\), and deterministic positive quadrature can annihilate all these moments. Thus no local zero-side positivity, counting, or quartet-symmetry argument can prove Gate 1.

---

## 3. Explicit-formula probes

For each \(k\in[3,R+3]\), choose a packet-adapted test function

\[
h_{k,\lambda,m_0}(t)
=
\lambda^{-1} h_k\!\left(\frac{t-m_0}{\lambda}\right),
\]

with \(h_k\) chosen so that its zero-side local contribution extracts the \(k\)-th packet sine moment:

\[
\sum_{\rho\in{\rm packet}} h_{k,\lambda,m_0}(\gamma_\rho)
=
c_k \sum_j w_j\sin(k\theta_j)
+
O({\rm chart\ error}).
\]

The Weil/Riemann explicit formula gives schematically

\[
\sum_\rho h_{k,\lambda,m_0}(\gamma_\rho)
=
\mathcal G_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
-
2\Re
\sum_{n\ge 1}
\frac{\Lambda(n)}{\sqrt n}
n^{-im_0}
\widehat h_k(\lambda\log n)
+
\mathcal T_{k,\lambda}(m_0),
\]

where:

\[
\mathcal G_{k,\lambda}
\]

contains gamma, pole, and trivial-zero terms, and

\[
\mathcal T_{k,\lambda}
\]

contains tail / truncation / regularization effects.

Define the prime wavelet coefficient

\[
\mathcal P_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
:=
2\Re
\sum_{n\ge 1}
\frac{\Lambda(n)}{\sqrt n}
n^{-im_0}
\widehat h_k(\lambda\log n).
\]

Then source flattening implies

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal P_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
=
\mathcal G_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
+
\mathcal T_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
+
O(\varepsilon),
\qquad 3\le k\le R+3.
}
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{k=3}^{R+3}
\left|
\mathcal P_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
-
\mathcal G_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
-
\mathcal T_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
\right|^2
\ll
R\varepsilon^2.
}
\]

This is the exact zero-prime compatibility condition.

---

## 4. Why this is not yet a contradiction

The explicit formula is an identity. It does not by itself say the prime-side vector cannot be small.

The bad event says that the actual prime coefficients \(\Lambda(n)\), sampled through the packet wavelets

\[
\widehat h_k(\lambda\log n),
\]

conspire so that a vector of \(R+1\) real oscillatory sums matches the smooth canonical background.

That is abnormal-looking, but proving it impossible is precisely a prime-side nonrecurrence theorem.

The zero packet has already been shown capable of positive same-scale finite quadrature. The explicit formula only transfers that burden to the prime side.

So the attempted theorem

\[
\text{same-scale zero source-flattening}
\Rightarrow
\text{prime/AFE imbalance}
\]

can only be proved if one establishes a lower bound of the following kind:

\[
\boxed{
\max_{3\le k\le R+3}
\left|
\mathcal P_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
-
\mathcal G_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
-
\mathcal T_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
\right|
\ge Q^{-B}.
}
\]

But this is equivalent in substance to

\[
\boxed{
\max_{0\le r\le R}
\left|
\partial_x^rF_\lambda(0)
\right|
\ge Q^{-B},
}
\]

or, in physical variables,

\[
\boxed{
\max_{0\le r\le R}
\left|
\Delta^r\partial_m^r q''_{\rm can}(m_0)
\right|
\ge Q^{-B}.
}
\]

That is the Agent 2 prime/AFE nonrecurrence theorem in wavelet/moment coordinates.

---

## 5. Candidate coercive quantity: packet spectral leakage

The most promising Agent 3 reformulation is not a pure zero theorem, but a **spectral leakage lower bound**.

Define

\[
\mathfrak L_R(m_0,\lambda)
:=
\sum_{k=3}^{R+3}
\left|
\mathcal P_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
-
\mathcal G_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
-
\mathcal T_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
\right|^2.
\]

A useful theorem would be:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\mathfrak L_R(m_0,\lambda)
\ge Q^{-B}.
}
\]

This would kill same-scale anti-design because source flattening gives

\[
\mathfrak L_R(m_0,\lambda)\ll R\varepsilon^2.
\]

A ratio version may be more stable:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{k=3}^{R+3}
\left|
\mathcal P_{k,\lambda}
-
\mathcal G_{k,\lambda}
-
\mathcal T_{k,\lambda}
\right|^2
\ge
Q^{-B}
\left|
\mathcal P_{1,\lambda}
-
\mathcal G_{1,\lambda}
-
\mathcal T_{1,\lambda}
\right|^2.
}
\]

Interpretation: actual zeta primes cannot put all packet-scale mass into the low harmonic mode while suppressing every curvature-visible mode \(k\ge 3\).

This is a cleaner version of the anti-design obstruction:

\[
\text{finite zero quadrature}
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\text{prime spectral leakage failure}.
\]

But proving this still requires fixed-coefficient arithmetic input about \(\Lambda(n)\). It is not available from the explicit formula alone.

---

## 6. A no-go for purely zero-side certificates

Suppose one tries to find coefficients \(a_k\), \(3\le k\le R+3\), such that

\[
\sum_{k=3}^{R+3} a_k
\sum_j w_j\sin(k\theta_j)
\]

has a definite sign or size for every admissible same-scale packet.

This cannot work, because deterministic positive sine quadrature gives

\[
\sum_j w_j\sin(k\theta_j)=0,
\qquad 3\le k\le R+3,
\]

while preserving positive mass in the low mode:

\[
\sum_j w_j\sin\theta_j=1.
\]

Therefore every linear functional using only curvature-visible modes \(k\ge3\) can be annihilated by a positive same-scale configuration.

Including \(k=1\) or \(k=2\) does not directly help, because those modes correspond to mass / affine / lower-order nuisance data, not to \(q''_{\rm can}\)-curvature observability. They do not supply Gate 1 unless one proves a separate coupling theorem forcing leakage from \(k=1,2\) into \(k\ge3\). That coupling is again an arithmetic prime/AFE theorem.

---

## 7. Proof skeleton for the reduction theorem

A rigorous Agent 3 reduction should proceed as follows.

### Step 1: Build packet probes

Construct \(h_{k,\lambda,m_0}\) so that, in the packet chart,

\[
\sum_{\rho\in{\rm packet}} h_{k,\lambda,m_0}(\gamma_\rho)
=
c_k M_k^{\rm packet}
+
O(Q^{-C_{\rm chart}}),
\]

where

\[
M_k^{\rm packet}
=
\sum_j w_j\sin(k\theta_j).
\]

### Step 2: Separate zero side

Write

\[
\sum_\rho h_{k,\lambda,m_0}(\gamma_\rho)
=
c_k M_k^{\rm packet}
+
M_k^{\rm far}
+
M_k^{\rm tail}
+
M_k^{\rm crit/reg}.
\]

Absorb all non-packet terms into

\[
\mathcal B_k
:=
M_k^{\rm far}
+
M_k^{\rm tail}
+
M_k^{\rm crit/reg}.
\]

Then source flattening gives

\[
c_k M_k^{\rm packet}+\mathcal B_k=O(\varepsilon).
\]

### Step 3: Apply explicit formula

Use

\[
\sum_\rho h_{k,\lambda,m_0}(\gamma_\rho)
=
\mathcal G_{k,\lambda}
-
\mathcal P_{k,\lambda}
+
\mathcal E_{k,\lambda}.
\]

Therefore

\[
\mathcal P_{k,\lambda}
-
\mathcal G_{k,\lambda}
-
\mathcal E_{k,\lambda}
=
O(\varepsilon).
\]

### Step 4: State equivalent prime obstruction

Thus Gate 1 follows if one proves

\[
\max_{3\le k\le R+3}
\left|
\mathcal P_{k,\lambda}
-
\mathcal G_{k,\lambda}
-
\mathcal E_{k,\lambda}
\right|
\ge Q^{-B}.
\]

This is the prime spectral leakage theorem.

### Step 5: Identify collapse

But the last theorem is exactly a fixed-coefficient prime/AFE nonrecurrence statement, merely expressed in a zero-adapted wavelet basis.

So Agent 3 does not avoid Agent 2. It gives Agent 2 a better coordinate system.

---

## 8. Recommended handoff to Agent 2 / Agent 6

For Agent 2, the sharper prime-side object is:

\[
\mathcal P_{k,\lambda}(m_0)
=
2\Re
\sum_{n\ge1}
\frac{\Lambda(n)}{\sqrt n}
n^{-im_0}
\widehat h_k(\lambda\log n),
\qquad 3\le k\le R+3.
\]

The target lower bound is:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\sum_{k=3}^{R+3}
\left|
\mathcal P_{k,\lambda}
-
\mathcal G_{k,\lambda}
-
\mathcal T_{k,\lambda}
\right|^2
\ge Q^{-B}.
}
\]

For Agent 6, the audit question is whether the probes \(h_{k,\lambda,m_0}\) actually recover the full canonical object

\[
q''_{\rm can}=\Phi_\zeta'''
\]

rather than a proxy zero kernel. In particular, the reduction is only valid after controlling:

\[
B_2,\quad D_2,\quad E_2,\quad
\text{critical-line regularization},\quad
\text{tail symmetry},\quad
\text{zero-side/AFE agreement}.
\]

---

## Final Agent 3 verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Same-scale zero anti-design does not itself force an explicit-formula contradiction.}
}
\]

It forces a finite vector of packet-adapted actual-prime wavelet coefficients to be abnormally small. Proving that this cannot happen is a promising formulation, but it is not an independent zero-side argument.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Agent 3 route collapses to Agent 2 prime/AFE nonrecurrence,}
}
\]

with the useful refinement that the right prime-side theorem should be framed as a **packet spectral leakage lower bound** rather than as a generic Dirichlet-polynomial transversality statement.

## Assistant (2026-04-26 18:55:03)

I’ll handle only Agent 3 / C. The focus is narrower than the prior zero-prime lane: whether the AFE main/dual split itself adds enough rigidity to prevent finite-jet source flattening.

_Thought for 1m 13s_

## Agent 3 / C — Main/dual AFE incompatibility

I am executing the **Agent 3 / C** instruction: test whether the AFE main and dual terms themselves prevent source-flat finite jets for the canonical object

\[
q''_{\rm can}=B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2.
\]

The relevant sprint instruction is in the uploaded coordinator message. 

## 1. Setup

Write the differentiated canonical AFE near \(m=m_0\) as

\[
q''_{\rm can}(m)
=
B_2(m)+P_2(m)+D_2(m)+E_2(m),
\]

with

\[
P_2(m)
=
\Re\sum_{n\le X}
a_n(m)e^{-im\log n},
\]

and

\[
D_2(m)
=
\Re\sum_{n\le Y}
b_n(m)e^{i\Theta(m)}e^{im\log n}.
\]

Here \(\Theta(m)\) is the gamma / functional-equation phase. In the standard critical-line normalization,

\[
\Theta'(m)
=
-\log\frac{m}{2\pi}
+
O(m^{-1}).
\]

Let

\[
T=\frac{m_0}{2\pi},
\qquad
XY\asymp T.
\]

Then the main effective frequencies are

\[
\omega_n^P=-\log n,
\]

while the dual effective frequencies are

\[
\omega_n^D
=
\Theta'(m_0)+\log n
=
\log n-\log T+O(m_0^{-1})
=
-\log(T/n)+O(m_0^{-1}).
\]

So the nominal sign difference between \(n^{-im}\) and \(n^{+im}\) is misleading. After the gamma phase is included, the dual term is not an independent opposite-sign copy. It is a complementary frequency block.

Equivalently,

\[
D_2
\quad\text{behaves like frequencies}\quad
-\log u,
\qquad
u\asymp T/n.
\]

Thus \(P_2+D_2\) is closer to a single folded frequency expansion over two complementary ranges than to two incompatible oscillatory systems.

---

## 2. Differentiated jets

For \(0\le r\le R\), ignoring lower-order coefficient derivatives for the moment,

\[
\partial_m^r P_2(m_0)
=
\Re\sum_{n\le X}
a_n(m_0)
(-i\log n)^r
e^{-im_0\log n}
+
{\rm l.o.t.},
\]

and

\[
\partial_m^r D_2(m_0)
=
\Re\sum_{n\le Y}
b_n(m_0)
i^r
\bigl(\Theta'(m_0)+\log n\bigr)^r
e^{i\Theta(m_0)+im_0\log n}
+
{\rm l.o.t.}.
\]

With the normalized jet operator \(\Delta^r\partial_m^r\),

\[
\Delta^r\partial_m^r P_2(m_0)
=
\Re\sum_{n\le X}
a_n(m_0)
(-i\Delta\log n)^r
e^{-im_0\log n}
+
{\rm l.o.t.},
\]

\[
\Delta^r\partial_m^r D_2(m_0)
=
\Re\sum_{n\le Y}
b_n(m_0)
(i\Delta\omega_n^D)^r
e^{i\Theta(m_0)+im_0\log n}
+
{\rm l.o.t.}.
\]

The bad Gate 1 jet condition is

\[
\Delta^r\partial_m^r(B_2+P_2+D_2)(m_0)
\approx0,
\qquad
0\le r\le R.
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\Re\sum_{n\le X}
A_n(\Delta\omega_n^P)^r
+
\Re\sum_{n\le Y}
C_n(\Delta\omega_n^D)^r
\approx
-\Delta^r\partial_m^r B_2(m_0),
\]

where

\[
A_n=a_n(m_0)e^{-im_0\log n},
\qquad
C_n=b_n(m_0)e^{i\Theta(m_0)+im_0\log n}.
\]

This is a finite moment-cancellation system.

---

## 3. Does main/dual opposition create overdetermination?

No, not by itself.

The dual frequencies satisfy

\[
\omega_n^D
\approx
-\log(T/n),
\]

so if \(XY\asymp T\), the main and dual blocks approximately partition the frequency range

\[
-\log u,
\qquad
1\lesssim u\lesssim T.
\]

For instance, in a symmetric AFE with \(X=Y\asymp T^{1/2}\),

\[
\omega_n^P\in[-\tfrac12\log T,0],
\]

while

\[
\omega_n^D\in[-\log T,-\tfrac12\log T].
\]

They meet at the saddle scale. There is no genuine opposite-sign incompatibility after the gamma phase is included.

Thus the bad jet system is not

\[
\text{main moment vector}
\approx0
\quad\text{and}\quad
\text{dual moment vector}
\approx0
\]

separately. It is

\[
\text{main moment vector}
+
\text{dual moment vector}
\approx
-\text{background vector}.
\]

That is underdetermined at the level of formal frequencies. The dual term increases the available cancellation space rather than immediately overdetermining it.

So the first verdict is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Main/dual opposition alone does not prove finite-jet anti-design.}
}
\]

---

## 4. Attempted differential-operator separation

Let

\[
L=p(\Delta\partial_m)
=
\sum_{r=0}^{R}c_r(\Delta\partial_m)^r.
\]

On a frozen-frequency exponential,

\[
e^{i\omega m},
\]

one has

\[
L e^{i\omega m}
=
p(i\Delta\omega)e^{i\omega m}.
\]

The hope would be to choose \(p\) so that

\[
p(i\Delta\omega_n^D)\approx0
\]

on the dual block, but

\[
p(i\Delta\omega_n^P)
\not\approx0
\]

on a main block. Then the bad jet condition would imply

\[
L P_2(m_0)
\approx
-L B_2(m_0),
\]

up to small dual and error terms. If one could prove

\[
|L P_2(m_0)+L B_2(m_0)|
\ge Q^{-B},
\]

Gate 1 would follow.

This is the most plausible main/dual route.

However, there are three obstructions.

### Obstruction 1: no clean frequency gap at the saddle

The main and dual frequency blocks meet at

\[
u\asymp X,
\qquad
T/n\asymp X.
\]

A polynomial \(p\) can separate intervals only away from the shared boundary. Near the saddle, suppressing \(D_2\) also suppresses or distorts part of \(P_2\).

If one sacrifices the saddle region and keeps only a low-frequency main block, the theorem becomes a lower bound for a restricted prime block, not a consequence of main/dual incompatibility.

### Obstruction 2: degree \(R\) filters amplify errors

A filter strong enough to give

\[
|p(i\Delta\omega_D)|\le Q^{-C}
\]

on a whole dual interval typically has coefficients of size

\[
\exp(O(R))
\]

or worse, depending on the separation geometry.

Then the error term must satisfy something like

\[
|L E_2(m_0)|
\le
Q^{-B},
\]

which is stronger than ordinary undifferentiated AFE control. The lower-order terms from differentiating \(a_n(m)\), \(b_n(m)\), and \(\Theta^{(j)}(m)\) also become non-negligible unless a full \(R\)-jet AFE error budget is proved.

### Obstruction 3: after filtering, one still needs prime nonrecurrence

Even if \(L\) suppresses \(D_2\), the remaining claim is

\[
|L P_2(m_0)+L B_2(m_0)|\ge Q^{-B}.
\]

But

\[
L P_2(m_0)
=
\Re\sum_{n\le X}
a_n(m_0)
p(-i\Delta\log n)
e^{-im_0\log n}
+
{\rm l.o.t.}
\]

This is a fixed-coefficient prime-phase lower bound. It is exactly the Agent 2-style nonrecurrence problem, only after applying a spectral multiplier.

So the operator route does not close Gate 1 unless one proves a prime-block lower bound.

---

## 5. Conditional theorem that would work

A viable theorem would look like this.

Assume there exists a polynomial \(p\) of degree \(\le R\) and a prime block \(\mathcal B\subseteq[1,X]\) such that:

\[
|p(i\Delta\omega_n^D)|\le Q^{-C}
\quad
\text{for all relevant dual }n,
\]

\[
|p(-i\Delta\log n)|\ge Q^{-C_p}
\quad
\text{for }n\in\mathcal B,
\]

and

\[
\left|
\Re\sum_{n\in\mathcal B}
a_n(m_0)
p(-i\Delta\log n)
e^{-im_0\log n}
+
L B_2(m_0)
\right|
\ge Q^{-B}.
\]

Assume also

\[
|L E_2(m_0)|\le Q^{-B-10},
\]

and the differentiated coefficient/phase errors are \(O(Q^{-B-10})\).

Then finite-jet source flattening is impossible.

Indeed, if

\[
\Delta^r\partial_m^r q''_{\rm can}(m_0)\approx0,
\qquad
0\le r\le R,
\]

then

\[
Lq''_{\rm can}(m_0)\approx0.
\]

But

\[
Lq''_{\rm can}
=
LB_2+LP_2+LD_2+LE_2.
\]

The dual and error terms are small by construction, so

\[
LB_2+LP_2\approx0,
\]

contradicting the assumed prime-block lower bound.

This theorem is mathematically clean, but its nontrivial hypothesis is the prime-block lower bound. Therefore it does not prove Gate 1 from AFE main/dual incompatibility alone.

---

## 6. Can \(L\) suppress \(D_2\) while keeping \(P_2\)?

Partially, but not enough.

If one works away from the saddle, a Chebyshev-type polynomial can separate two compact frequency intervals. For example, if

\[
\Omega_P\cap\Omega_D=\varnothing
\]

with separation \(\delta>0\), one can build \(p\) of degree \(R\) with rough behavior

\[
|p|_{\Omega_D}\le e^{-cR\delta},
\qquad
|p|_{\Omega_P}\ge1.
\]

So with

\[
R\asymp C\log Q,
\]

one can get \(Q^{-C'}\)-level dual suppression.

But in the actual AFE split, the intervals touch at the saddle. So one must either:

1. discard a transition region, or  
2. accept poor constants, or  
3. use an unbalanced AFE and move the problem to another boundary.

None gives a free contradiction.

At best, this produces a conditional reduction:

\[
\text{Gate 1 bad jets}
\Rightarrow
\text{one filtered actual-prime block is anomalously small}.
\]

That is useful, but still a prime arithmetic theorem.

---

## 7. Main/dual coupling mostly shifts the source-flat target

The bad condition

\[
\Delta^r\partial_m^r(B_2+P_2+D_2)(m_0)\approx0
\]

can be rewritten as

\[
\Delta^r\partial_m^rP_2(m_0)
\approx
-\Delta^r\partial_m^r(B_2+D_2)(m_0).
\]

So the dual term does not force \(P_2\) to be small. It gives \(P_2\) a target vector to hit.

The question becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can the fixed actual prime phases hit the finite jet target generated by }B_2+D_2?
}
\]

That is not a main/dual incompatibility theorem. It is finite prime-phase recurrence with a moving deterministic target.

Equivalently, after folding the dual block into complementary frequencies, the bad event is just

\[
\Re\sum_{u\in\mathcal U}
C_u(m_0)
(\Delta\omega_u)^r
e^{-im_0\log u}
\approx
-\Delta^r\partial_m^rB_2(m_0),
\qquad
0\le r\le R,
\]

where \(\mathcal U\) contains the main frequencies and the dual complementary frequencies. This is a larger finite moment system, not an overdetermined one.

---

## 8. Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Main/dual AFE incompatibility does not currently close Gate 1.}
}
\]

More precisely:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The nominal opposite signs of }P_2\text{ and }D_2
\text{ do not create an intrinsic finite-jet contradiction.}
}
\]

After including the gamma phase,

\[
D_2
\]

is a complementary frequency block, not an independent opposite-frequency copy. The bad source-flat system becomes a finite moment cancellation problem for the combined main-plus-dual AFE expansion.

The only promising use of main/dual structure is a **filtered reduction**:

\[
\text{bad jets}
\Rightarrow
\text{filtered actual-prime block is anomalously small}.
\]

But proving that filtered block cannot be small is still a fixed-coefficient prime nonrecurrence theorem.

So the Agent 3 conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Main/dual coupling helps define better prime-side tests,}
\quad
\text{but it does not by itself prove finite-jet anti-design.}
}
\]

The clean handoff is to Agent 2 / Agent 1:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Prove that the actual prime orbit cannot hit the }B_2+D_2
\text{ finite-jet target within }Q^{-B}.
}
\]
## User (2026-04-26 18:59:26)

Do the agent instruction in the message as per your agent number.

## Assistant (2026-04-26 18:59:27)

I’ll narrow this to the filtered AFE route: construct the best possible \(L=p(\Delta\partial_m)\), quantify what it buys, then identify the remaining theorem.

_Thought for 12s_

## Agent 3 / C — Main/dual filtered prime-block theorem

I am executing the **Agent 3 / C** instruction from the uploaded coordinator prompt: refine the main/dual filtered-prime-block route for

\[
q''_{\rm can}=B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2.
\]

The assigned task is specifically to test whether a differential operator

\[
L=p(\Delta\partial_m)
\]

can suppress one AFE block while preserving the other, and whether this yields a genuinely easier Gate 1 theorem. 

---

# 1. Effective AFE frequencies

Let

\[
T=\frac{m_0}{2\pi}.
\]

Use a schematic AFE splitting

\[
P_2(m)
=
\Re\sum_{n\le X}
a_n(m)e^{-im\log n},
\]

\[
D_2(m)
=
\Re\sum_{n\le Y}
b_n(m)e^{i\Theta(m)}e^{im\log n},
\]

with

\[
XY\asymp T.
\]

The main frequencies are

\[
\omega_n^P=-\log n.
\]

For the dual term,

\[
\omega_n^D
=
\Theta'(m_0)+\log n.
\]

The functional-equation phase satisfies

\[
\Theta'(m_0)
=
-\log T+O(T^{-1}),
\]

so

\[
\omega_n^D
=
\log n-\log T+O(T^{-1})
=
-\log(T/n)+O(T^{-1}).
\]

Thus the dual block is not an opposite-frequency mirror of the main block. It is a complementary folded block.

If \(X=T^\sigma\), \(Y=T^{1-\sigma}\), then approximately

\[
\Omega_P=[-\sigma\log T,0],
\]

\[
\Omega_D=[-\log T,-\sigma\log T].
\]

For the symmetric AFE \(X=Y=T^{1/2}\),

\[
\Omega_D=[-\log T,-\tfrac12\log T],
\qquad
\Omega_P=[-\tfrac12\log T,0].
\]

They touch at the saddle frequency

\[
-\log X.
\]

This is the central obstruction to a clean main/dual separation.

---

# 2. What the bad jet condition says

The bad Gate 1 jet condition is

\[
\Delta^r\partial_m^r(B_2+P_2+D_2)(m_0)\approx0,
\qquad
0\le r\le R.
\]

Freezing coefficient derivatives for the first pass,

\[
\Delta^r\partial_m^r P_2(m_0)
\approx
\Re\sum_{n\le X}
a_n(m_0)
(i\Delta\omega_n^P)^r
e^{i\omega_n^P m_0},
\]

and

\[
\Delta^r\partial_m^r D_2(m_0)
\approx
\Re\sum_{n\le Y}
b_n(m_0)
(i\Delta\omega_n^D)^r
e^{i\Theta(m_0)+im_0\log n}.
\]

Therefore the bad locus is a finite moment-cancellation system:

\[
\Re\sum_{n\le X}
A_n(i\Delta\omega_n^P)^r
+
\Re\sum_{n\le Y}
C_n(i\Delta\omega_n^D)^r
\approx
-\Delta^r\partial_m^r B_2(m_0).
\]

This is not overdetermined merely because \(P_2\) and \(D_2\) coexist. The dual block gives additional cancellation degrees of freedom.

---

# 3. Differential filter model

Let

\[
L=p(\Delta\partial_m),
\qquad
p(z)=\sum_{r=0}^R c_r z^r.
\]

On a frozen exponential \(e^{i\omega m}\),

\[
L e^{i\omega m}=p(i\Delta\omega)e^{i\omega m}.
\]

So

\[
LP_2(m_0)
\approx
\Re\sum_{n\le X}
a_n(m_0)
p(i\Delta\omega_n^P)
e^{i\omega_n^P m_0},
\]

\[
LD_2(m_0)
\approx
\Re\sum_{n\le Y}
b_n(m_0)
p(i\Delta\omega_n^D)
e^{i\Theta(m_0)+im_0\log n}.
\]

The desired filter is:

\[
|p(i\Delta\omega)|\le Q^{-C}
\quad\text{for }\omega\in\Omega_D^{\rm kept},
\]

while

\[
|p(i\Delta\omega)|\ge Q^{-C'}
\quad\text{for }\omega\in\Omega_P^{\rm kept}.
\]

If such \(p\) exists with controlled coefficient norm, then bad jets imply

\[
L(B_2+P_2)(m_0)
\approx0,
\]

because \(LD_2\) and \(LE_2\) are small. One would then need

\[
|L(B_2+P_2)(m_0)|\ge Q^{-B}.
\]

This is the filtered prime-block theorem.

---

# 4. Frequency gap analysis

There is no global gap between \(\Omega_P\) and \(\Omega_D\), because they touch at the saddle.

However, there is a gap after discarding a transition band.

For symmetric \(X=Y=T^{1/2}\), write

\[
u=\frac{\omega}{\log T}.
\]

Then

\[
\Omega_D/\log T=[-1,-1/2],
\qquad
\Omega_P/\log T=[-1/2,0].
\]

Fix \(\eta>0\). Define separated blocks

\[
\Omega_D^\eta=[-\log T,(-1/2-\eta)\log T],
\]

\[
\Omega_P^\eta=[(-1/2+\eta)\log T,0].
\]

The normalized gap is \(2\eta\).

A Chebyshev-type polynomial filter of degree \(R\) can suppress one interval while preserving the other with attenuation roughly

\[
\exp(-cR\eta)
\]

in the normalized variable.

Thus to get

\[
Q^{-C}
\]

suppression, one needs

\[
R\eta\gtrsim C\log Q.
\]

Therefore:

- if \(R\asymp \log Q\), one may take \(\eta\asymp 1\);
- if \(R\asymp \log Q/\log\log Q\), then one needs \(\eta\asymp \log\log Q\), impossible as a normalized gap;
- alternatively, the suppression is only

\[
\exp\!\left(-c\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q}\right)
=
Q^{-c/\log\log Q},
\]

which is sub-polynomial, not \(Q^{-C}\).

This is a significant constraint.

### Consequence

The filtered main/dual separation is viable only in one of three regimes:

\[
\boxed{R\asymp \log Q}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{\text{we accept sub-polynomial dual suppression}}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{\text{we use a much wider sacrificed saddle band and prove mass remains outside it}.}
\]

The last option requires an additional prime-block mass theorem.

---

# 5. Coefficient growth and error amplification

A degree-\(R\) polynomial filter with Chebyshev-type transition has coefficient norm at least exponential in \(R\):

\[
\|p\|_{\rm coeff}\le \exp(CR)
\]

in a normalized coordinate. Undoing the normalization introduces powers of

\[
(\Delta\log T)^{-1}
\]

depending on the basis.

Thus one expects a bound of the form

\[
|LE_2(m_0)|
\le
\sum_{r=0}^R |c_r|\Delta^r|\partial_m^rE_2(m_0)|.
\]

To make the filtered theorem useful, one needs

\[
|LE_2(m_0)|\le Q^{-B-10}.
\]

A sufficient AFE error budget is therefore

\[
\Delta^r|\partial_m^rE_2(m_0)|
\le
Q^{-B-C R}
\]

for all \(0\le r\le R\), after normalization.

This is mild if

\[
R\asymp \frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q}
\]

and if the AFE derivative constants are \(e^{O(R\log R)}\), because

\[
e^{O(R\log R)}=Q^{O(1)}.
\]

It becomes dangerous if

\[
R\asymp \log Q
\]

and the derivative constants are \(e^{O(R\log R)}\), because then

\[
e^{O(R\log R)}
=
Q^{O(\log\log Q)},
\]

which is not polynomial in \(Q\).

So there is a tension:

\[
R\asymp\log Q
\]

helps filtering, but hurts canonical derivative/error control.

Meanwhile,

\[
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q}
\]

keeps derivative complexity plausible, but gives too weak a filter unless a real frequency gap or mass localization theorem is added.

---

# 6. Candidate filtered theorem

A clean conditional theorem is the following.

## Filtered prime-block anti-design theorem

Let \(R\) be the finite-jet depth. Suppose there exists a polynomial \(p\) of degree \(\le R\) and separated frequency blocks

\[
\mathcal B_P\subseteq\{n\le X\},
\qquad
\mathcal B_D\subseteq\{n\le Y\},
\]

such that:

### Passband on a main block

\[
|p(i\Delta\omega_n^P)|\ge Q^{-C_p},
\qquad n\in\mathcal B_P.
\]

### Stopband on the relevant dual block

\[
|p(i\Delta\omega_n^D)|\le Q^{-C_s},
\qquad n\in\mathcal B_D.
\]

### Residual blocks are negligible

The discarded saddle and tail pieces satisfy

\[
|L(P_2-P_{2,\mathcal B_P})(m_0)|
+
|L(D_2-D_{2,\mathcal B_D})(m_0)|
\le Q^{-B-10}.
\]

### Canonical error controlled

\[
|LE_2(m_0)|\le Q^{-B-10}.
\]

### Filtered prime lower bound

\[
\boxed{
|L(B_2+P_{2,\mathcal B_P})(m_0)|
\ge Q^{-B}.
}
\]

Then the bad finite-jet condition

\[
\Delta^r\partial_m^r q''_{\rm can}(m_0)\approx0,
\qquad 0\le r\le R,
\]

is impossible.

### Proof

If all jets vanish to tolerance \(\varepsilon\), then

\[
Lq''_{\rm can}(m_0)=O(\|p\|_1\varepsilon).
\]

But

\[
Lq''_{\rm can}
=
L(B_2+P_{2,\mathcal B_P})
+
LD_{2,\mathcal B_D}
+
L(P_2-P_{2,\mathcal B_P})
+
L(D_2-D_{2,\mathcal B_D})
+
LE_2.
\]

The stopband, residual, and error assumptions make all terms except

\[
L(B_2+P_{2,\mathcal B_P})
\]

smaller than \(Q^{-B-10}\). Hence

\[
|Lq''_{\rm can}(m_0)|
\ge
Q^{-B}-O(Q^{-B-10}),
\]

contradicting source-flat jets for sufficiently small \(\varepsilon\).

---

# 7. Is the filtered theorem easier than original nonrecurrence?

Partially, but not decisively.

The original theorem asks for

\[
\max_{0\le r\le R}
|\Delta^r\partial_m^r q''_{\rm can}(m_0)|
\ge Q^{-B}.
\]

The filtered theorem asks for one lower bound:

\[
|L(B_2+P_{2,\mathcal B_P})(m_0)|\ge Q^{-B}.
\]

This is simpler in one sense: it reduces the vector anti-design theorem to a scalar prime-block lower bound.

But it is not obviously easier arithmetically. The scalar quantity is still a fixed-coefficient Dirichlet polynomial:

\[
L P_{2,\mathcal B_P}(m_0)
=
\Re
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_P}
a_n(m_0)
p(-i\Delta\log n)
e^{-im_0\log n}
+
{\rm l.o.t.}
\]

Thus one must still prove that this actual prime block does not hit the moving target

\[
-LB_2(m_0).
\]

That is a fixed-coefficient prime nonrecurrence theorem.

The filter improves the geometry by removing dual cancellation, but it does not remove the hard arithmetic lower bound.

---

# 8. Strongest plausible use of this route

The route becomes promising if combined with a block where the coefficients are sign-structured or dominated.

For example, if one can isolate a short block \(\mathcal B_P\) such that:

\[
a_n(m_0)p(-i\Delta\log n)
\]

has slowly varying argument or a dominant prime contribution, then

\[
|LP_{2,\mathcal B_P}(m_0)|
\]

may have a direct lower bound.

But without such dominance, phase cancellation remains possible.

The most plausible strengthened theorem is therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Filtered separated block}
+
\text{actual-prime coefficient rigidity}
+
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
|L(B_2+P_{2,\mathcal B_P})(m_0)|\ge Q^{-B}.
}
\]

The \(Z_\alpha\)-condition must enter here; otherwise the filtered block can still recur too cheaply by the volume heuristic.

---

# 9. No-go diagnosis

The pure main/dual filter route fails as a standalone proof for three reasons.

## First: no full spectral gap

\[
\Omega_P
\]

and

\[
\Omega_D
\]

touch at the saddle. Any separation sacrifices a transition band.

## Second: filter strength conflicts with derivative budget

To obtain polynomial \(Q^{-C}\) suppression, one wants

\[
R\asymp\log Q.
\]

But canonical Wronskian / AFE derivative control is more plausible at

\[
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q}.
\]

At that smaller \(R\), Chebyshev filtering gives only sub-polynomial suppression unless additional separation is available.

## Third: the remaining lower bound is still prime nonrecurrence

Even after suppressing \(D_2\), the key lower bound is

\[
|L(P_2+B_2)(m_0)|\ge Q^{-B}.
\]

This is not automatic from AFE structure. It is the same fixed actual-zeta arithmetic problem in a scalar filtered form.

---

# 10. Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The main/dual filtered route is Yellow, not Green.}
}
\]

It gives a useful conditional reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{bad finite jets}
\Rightarrow
\text{filtered actual-prime block hits a small target}.
}
\]

But it does not independently prove Gate 1.

The route would become viable only if one proves an additional theorem of the following type:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
|L(B_2+P_{2,\mathcal B_P})(m_0)|
\ge Q^{-B}
}
\]

for a filter \(L\) with controlled dual suppression and canonical error budget.

So the refined Agent 3 conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Filtering improves the shape of the prime-side target,}
\quad
\text{but it merely shifts the hard theorem to a filtered prime-block nonrecurrence bound.}
}
\]
## User (2026-04-26 19:02:57)

Do the agent instruction in the message as per your agent number.

## Assistant (2026-04-26 19:02:58)

I’ll treat the determinant as a fixed exponential polynomial and test whether a polynomial lower bound can hold pointwise. The key issue is whether subset-product structure gives any sign, dominance, or Diophantine protection beyond generic recurrence.

_Thought for 33s_

## Agent 3 / C — Subset-product Wronskian nonrecurrence

I am executing the **Agent 3 / C** instruction: attack the missing theorem

\[
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

for the normalized canonical Wronskian

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)
=
\sum_S \widetilde C_S(\alpha)e^{-im\log N_S}.
\]

The coordinator prompt asks for a lower bound or obstruction for this subset-product determinant/nonrecurrence problem. 

---

# 1. Model of the determinant

Let

\[
s=R+2.
\]

The normalized Wronskian has the Cauchy--Binet expansion

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)
=
\sum_{\substack{S\subset\mathcal B\\ |S|=s}}
\widetilde C_S(\alpha)e^{-im\Lambda_S},
\]

where

\[
\Lambda_S=\log N_S=\sum_{n\in S}\log n.
\]

Equivalently,

\[
N_S=\prod_{n\in S}n.
\]

The coefficient has the schematic form

\[
\widetilde C_S(\alpha)
=
\left(\prod_{n\in S} c_n\right)
\det
\begin{pmatrix}
r_n(\alpha)\\
p_0(\Delta\log n)\\
\vdots\\
p_R(\Delta\log n)
\end{pmatrix}_{n\in S}
\cdot
\prod_{n<n'\in S}
\bigl(\Delta\log n'-\Delta\log n\bigr),
\]

where

\[
r_n(\alpha)=\frac{z_n(\alpha)}{c_n}.
\]

The \(Z_\alpha\)-symbol transversality result says that the row \(r_n(\alpha)\) is not polynomially generated by the \(q''_{\rm can}\)-jet rows. That implies the coefficients are not identically zero on a thick block.

But non-identically-zero is far weaker than

\[
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

The determinant is still a fixed exponential polynomial.

---

# 2. Effective number of active terms

Suppose the active block contains

\[
M=|\mathcal B|
\]

usable frequencies. Then the number of subset terms is

\[
T=\binom{M}{s}.
\]

For

\[
M\asymp cR
\]

with \(c>1\),

\[
T
=
\binom{cR}{R+2}
=
\exp\bigl(R H(1/c)+O(\log R)\bigr),
\]

so already exponentially many terms in \(R\).

For a thicker block,

\[
M\gg R,
\]

the term count is much larger:

\[
T\sim \frac{M^s}{s!}.
\]

The effective phase dimension is not \(T\), however. If the frequencies are supported on primes \(p\le P\), then all phases are generated by

\[
(e^{-im\log p})_{p\le P},
\]

so the torus dimension is roughly

\[
d\sim \pi(P)
\]

or, more precisely, the number of multiplicatively independent prime factors appearing in the active block.

For a deliberately small active prime block with

\[
M\asymp R,
\]

one has

\[
d\asymp R.
\]

For a thick arithmetic AFE block, \(d\) may be much larger.

This distinction matters: recurrence cost is governed by \(d\), not by \(T\).

---

# 3. Recurrence obstruction

Treat

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R
\]

as a trigonometric polynomial on the prime phase torus

\[
\mathbb T^d.
\]

If there exists a torus phase point \(\theta^\ast\) with

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(\theta^\ast,\alpha)=0,
\]

then Kronecker recurrence suggests that the one-parameter orbit

\[
m\mapsto (-m\log p_1,\ldots,-m\log p_d)
\]

hits an \(\varepsilon\)-neighborhood of that zero with recurrence time approximately

\[
\tau(\varepsilon)
\sim \varepsilon^{-d}
\]

up to Diophantine losses.

For the desired lower bound

\[
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R|\ge Q^{-A},
\]

take

\[
\varepsilon\asymp Q^{-A'}
\]

after dividing by a Lipschitz constant. Then

\[
\tau
\sim Q^{A'd}
=
\exp(A'd\log Q).
\]

If the active dimension can be kept at

\[
d\asymp R
\asymp
\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\]

then

\[
\tau
=
\exp\left(
O\left(\frac{(\log Q)^2}{\log\log Q}\right)
\right).
\]

This is far below a natural height budget of order

\[
m\le e^{Q}
\]

when \(Q\sim\log m\).

Therefore, in small active dimension, generic recurrence is too cheap to support a universal pointwise lower bound.

This is the same obstruction seen in the finite prime-phase recurrence analysis, but now applied to the subset-product Wronskian.

---

# 4. Existence of torus zeros in relaxed models

A pointwise lower bound could still survive if the torus function never approached zero. That would require something like coefficient dominance or positivity.

But generically, the determinant expansion has many comparable terms and no fixed sign.

A phase-relaxed countermodel is easy:

Take two subset terms \(S_1,S_2\) with comparable nonzero coefficients,

\[
|\widetilde C_{S_1}|\asymp |\widetilde C_{S_2}|.
\]

Hold all other phases fixed or arrange them small by cancellation. Then choose phases so that

\[
\widetilde C_{S_1}e^{-i\theta_{S_1}}
+
\widetilde C_{S_2}e^{-i\theta_{S_2}}
\approx 0.
\]

Because \(\theta_S\) is a sum of prime phases, this is achievable in a phase-relaxed torus model unless the two subset frequencies are arithmetically locked.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The determinant has no structural torus-level separation from zero.}
}
\]

Any lower bound must use actual one-parameter arithmetic, not only symbol rank.

---

# 5. Coefficient dominance check

A dominance proof would require a subset \(S_0\) such that

\[
|\widetilde C_{S_0}|
>
\sum_{S\ne S_0}|\widetilde C_S|
+
Q^{-A}.
\]

This appears unavailable on a thick active block.

Reason:

1. The AFE weights \(c_n\), \(z_n(\alpha)\), and smoothing factors vary slowly across a moderate block.
2. The normalized Vandermonde factors

   \[
   \prod_{n<n'\in S}|\Delta\log n'-\Delta\log n|
   \]

   vary, but not enough to dominate exponentially many neighboring subsets unless one chooses an extremely sparse or endpoint-heavy block.
3. The number of comparable subsets is exponential in \(R\).

At best, one might select a sparse block with \(M=s\), so the determinant has a single subset term. But then the Wronskian only tests a handcrafted minor, not the full canonical determinant. It also risks losing thick-block transversality and robustness under \(B_2,D_2,E_2\).

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No natural coefficient-dominance mechanism is visible for the full Wronskian.}
}
\]

---

# 6. Sign or phase rigidity check

One might hope that the determinant coefficients have common phase. They do not.

The coefficients contain:

\[
\det
\begin{pmatrix}
r_n(\alpha)\\
p_0(\Delta\log n)\\
\vdots\\
p_R(\Delta\log n)
\end{pmatrix},
\]

and the row \(r_n(\alpha)\) is generally complex or sign-varying once the exact AFE weights, gamma phase, dual terms, and smoothing are included.

Even in a simplified real model, the exponential factors

\[
e^{-im\log N_S}
\]

rotate independently along the prime-log orbit.

Thus there is no positivity comparable to

\[
\sum_S |\widetilde C_S|^2
\]

at the pointwise level.

A second-moment identity may hold, but it gives average mass, not pointwise exclusion of small values.

---

# 7. Subset-product spacing

The subset frequencies are

\[
\Lambda_S=\log N_S.
\]

If the \(n\)'s are distinct primes, then \(N_S\) are distinct and the frequencies are separated by

\[
|\log N_S-\log N_{S'}|
=
\left|\log\frac{N_S}{N_{S'}}\right|.
\]

This gives uniqueness of frequencies, and over long intervals

\[
\int_J |\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|^2\,dm
\]

approximately diagonalizes:

\[
\int_J |\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R|^2\,dm
\approx
|J|
\sum_S|\widetilde C_S|^2
\]

provided \(J\) is long compared with inverse frequency spacings.

This is useful but insufficient.

It gives an averaged lower bound:

\[
\frac1{|J|}
\int_J |\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R|^2\,dm
\gg
\sum_S|\widetilde C_S|^2.
\]

It does not imply

\[
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

at every admissible center \(m_0\).

To transfer average mass to pointwise lower bounds one would need an exclusion theorem saying admissible centers avoid the small-value set of \(\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\). That is another nonrecurrence theorem.

---

# 8. \(Z_\alpha\)-incidence exclusion

The best possible rescue is that admissible centers are not arbitrary. They satisfy

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0.
\]

So the required theorem is not globally

\[
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}
\quad\text{for all }m,
\]

but rather

\[
Z_\alpha(m,\alpha)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This is stronger structurally, but still not currently provable from determinant algebra.

In a phase-relaxed torus model, one can impose both

\[
Z_\alpha=0
\]

and

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R=0
\]

because there are many more phase degrees of freedom than scalar equations.

If \(\alpha\) is also variable, the incidence problem becomes still less rigid.

So symbol transversality gives:

\[
Z_\alpha
\notin
\operatorname{span}\{\mathcal D^j q''_{\rm can}\}_{j\le R},
\]

but it does **not** imply:

\[
\{Z_\alpha=0\}
\cap
\{\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R=0\}
=
\varnothing.
\]

The missing theorem is an arithmetic incidence exclusion:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the actual one-parameter zeta orbit does not hit }
\{Z_\alpha=0,\ \widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\approx0\}
\text{ within }Q^{-A}.
}
\]

That is plausible only with fixed actual-zeta arithmetic, not with torus or determinant algebra alone.

---

# 9. Strongest countermodel

A realistic surrogate countermodel is:

1. Choose an active prime block with

   \[
   d\asymp R.
   \]

2. Use exact-looking non-polynomial \(Z_\alpha\)-symbol ratios \(r_p(\alpha)\), so symbol transversality is real.

3. Form the normalized Wronskian determinant

   \[
   W(\theta,\alpha)
   =
   \sum_{|S|=s}
   \widetilde C_S(\alpha)e^{-i\sum_{p\in S}\theta_p}.
   \]

4. Because the coefficients are numerous and comparable, choose a torus phase point where

   \[
   W(\theta,\alpha)=0.
   \]

5. Also impose

   \[
   Z_\alpha(\theta)=0.
   \]

   In phase-relaxed dimension \(d\asymp R\), this is still underdetermined.

6. Use Kronecker recurrence to approximate this phase point along

   \[
   \theta_p=-m\log p
   \]

   within error \(Q^{-A'}\), at cost

   \[
   Q^{O(R)}
   =
   \exp\left(
   O\left(\frac{(\log Q)^2}{\log\log Q}\right)
   \right).
   \]

This is below the likely admissible height budget.

Therefore, in this surrogate,

\[
Z_\alpha(m)\approx0,
\qquad
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)\approx0
\]

are simultaneously recurrent.

This does not disprove the actual zeta theorem, because it ignores exact AFE/dual/gamma/tail compatibility across all active frequencies. But it shows the determinant route cannot be closed by generic torus dimension, symbol rank, or subset-product uniqueness.

---

# 10. What a positive theorem would need

A viable determinant lower-bound theorem must add one of the following mechanisms.

## 10.1 Arithmetic incidence exclusion

Prove that the actual orbit avoids the joint bad set:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m)=0
\Rightarrow
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This must use exact coefficients, including \(B_2,D_2,E_2\), not only abstract independent phases.

## 10.2 Dominant isolated minor

Find a canonical minor \(S_0\) such that

\[
|\widetilde C_{S_0}|
\gg
\sum_{S\ne S_0}|\widetilde C_S|
\]

after a legitimate local operation. I currently see no natural source for this.

## 10.3 Filtered determinant with local admissibility

Construct a local filter that isolates a subset family \(\mathfrak S_0\) while preserving the Gate 1 packet scale. This is more Agent 4’s lane. Without such filtering, the full determinant is too cancellation-prone.

## 10.4 Higher-order arithmetic rigidity

Show that whenever the determinant is small at a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero, the full AFE system forces additional identities that contradict zero-counting, Euler product structure, or the completed functional equation. This would be a genuine actual-zeta theorem.

---

# 11. Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Subset-product Wronskian nonrecurrence is unresolved, with a serious recurrence obstruction.}
}
\]

More specifically:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The determinant is not protected by coefficient dominance, positivity, or subset-product spacing.}
}
\]

The normalized Wronskian route remains possible only if one proves a genuinely arithmetic incidence theorem:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

for the actual canonical zeta object.

In relaxed prime-phase models, even with genuine \(Z_\alpha\)-symbol rank, the Wronskian can be made small by subset-product phase cancellation, and recurrence appears cheap when the active dimension is \(O(R)\).

So my Agent 3 conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No determinant lower bound is visible from the subset-product structure alone.}
}
\]

The next positive step would need to exploit exact actual-zeta compatibility, not generic Wronskian algebra.

## Assistant (2026-04-26 19:06:20)

_Thought for 10s_

## Agent 3 / C — Subset-product Wronskian nonrecurrence

I am executing the **Agent 3 / C** instruction from the coordinator prompt: attack the missing lower bound

\[
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

for the normalized Wronskian determinant

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)
=
\sum_S \widetilde C_S(\alpha)e^{-im\log N_S}.
\]

The prompt frames this as the current core obstruction in the normalized Wronskian route. 

---

# 1. Determinant as a torus polynomial

Let

\[
s=R+2.
\]

Restrict first to an active block of primes

\[
\mathcal P=\{p_1,\dots,p_M\}.
\]

Then each subset term is

\[
N_S=\prod_{p\in S}p,
\qquad |S|=s,
\]

and

\[
e^{-im\log N_S}
=
\prod_{p\in S}p^{-im}.
\]

Set

\[
z_p=p^{-im}\in S^1.
\]

Then the determinant becomes a homogeneous degree-\(s\) torus polynomial

\[
W(z,\alpha)
=
\sum_{\substack{S\subset\mathcal P\\ |S|=s}}
\widetilde C_S(\alpha)\prod_{p\in S}z_p.
\]

The one-parameter zeta orbit is

\[
m\mapsto z(m)
=
(p_1^{-im},\dots,p_M^{-im})
\in\mathbb T^M.
\]

Thus the desired pointwise theorem is

\[
Z_\alpha(m)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|W(z(m),\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This is not a formal determinant identity. It is an arithmetic avoidance theorem for a one-parameter orbit in a high-dimensional torus.

---

# 2. Size of the active determinant family

The number of subset terms is

\[
T=\binom{M}{s}.
\]

If

\[
M=cR,\qquad s=R+2,
\]

then

\[
T
=
\exp\{R H(1/c)+O(\log R)\}.
\]

If \(M\gg R\), then

\[
T\sim \frac{M^s}{s!}.
\]

The effective phase dimension is approximately

\[
d=M
\]

for a prime block. If the active terms include composite \(n\), then \(d\) is the number of primes appearing in their factorizations, but the same conclusion holds: the determinant is a polynomial on a prime-phase torus.

The recurrence cost is controlled by \(d\), not directly by \(T\).

For the preferred depth

\[
R\asymp \frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\]

and an economical active block

\[
d\asymp R,
\]

a \(Q^{-A}\)-small torus target has expected recurrence time

\[
Q^{O(d)}
=
\exp\left(
O\!\left(\frac{(\log Q)^2}{\log\log Q}\right)
\right).
\]

This is too cheap relative to any ambient height range of exponential size in \(Q\). Therefore plain phase recurrence cannot prove the desired lower bound.

---

# 3. Coefficient dominance is not available generically

A dominance proof would need some \(S_0\) satisfying

\[
|\widetilde C_{S_0}|
>
\sum_{S\ne S_0}|\widetilde C_S|
+
Q^{-A}.
\]

But the normalized coefficients have the schematic form

\[
\widetilde C_S(\alpha)
=
\left(\prod_{p\in S}c_p\right)
\det
\begin{pmatrix}
r_p(\alpha)\\
1\\
\Delta\log p\\
\vdots\\
(\Delta\log p)^R
\end{pmatrix}_{p\in S}
\cdot
\prod_{p<p'\in S}
(\Delta\log p'-\Delta\log p),
\]

where

\[
r_p(\alpha)=\frac{z_p(\alpha)}{c_p}.
\]

On a thick active block:

1. \(c_p\) varies slowly.
2. AFE smoothing weights vary slowly away from cutoffs.
3. The normalized Vandermonde changes substantially but not enough to dominate exponentially many nearby subsets.
4. The \(Z_\alpha\)-symbol row supplies rank, not positivity.

So the determinant has many comparable terms. No natural unique dominant subset is visible.

Conclusion:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Cauchy--Binet nonvanishing does not upgrade to pointwise lower bounds by dominance.}
}
\]

---

# 4. Sign / phase rigidity is absent

Even if the determinant coefficients were real in a simplified model, the factors

\[
e^{-im\log N_S}
\]

rotate with subset-product phases.

In the exact canonical setting, \(\widetilde C_S(\alpha)\) also inherits phase variation from:

\[
Z_\alpha,\qquad D_2,\qquad \text{gamma factors},\qquad \text{AFE smoothing}.
\]

Thus there is no positivity mechanism of the form

\[
W(z,\alpha)
=
\sum_S |\widetilde C_S| \cdot \text{positive term}.
\]

At best one gets an average identity:

\[
\int_J |W(m,\alpha)|^2\,dm
\approx
|J|\sum_S|\widetilde C_S(\alpha)|^2
\]

over sufficiently long \(J\), assuming subset-product frequencies are separated enough. But this only gives mean square mass. It does not prevent isolated or recurrent small values at admissible centers.

---

# 5. Subset-product spacing gives average mass, not pointwise exclusion

For prime subsets,

\[
\log N_S-\log N_{S'}
=
\log\frac{N_S}{N_{S'}}.
\]

Distinct subsets give distinct \(N_S\), hence distinct frequencies. This helps diagonalize long interval averages:

\[
\int_J
e^{-im(\log N_S-\log N_{S'})}\,dm
\]

is small when

\[
|J|\cdot |\log N_S-\log N_{S'}|\gg1.
\]

Therefore,

\[
\frac1{|J|}\int_J |W(m,\alpha)|^2\,dm
=
\sum_S|\widetilde C_S(\alpha)|^2
+
\text{off-diagonal error}.
\]

This proves \(W\) is not identically small on most long intervals.

It does not prove

\[
|W(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

at every \(m_0\) satisfying

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0.
\]

To get that, one needs an incidence-exclusion theorem:

\[
\{m:Z_\alpha(m)=0\}
\cap
\{m:|W(m,\alpha)|<Q^{-A}\}
=
\varnothing
\]

or at least a quantitative separation between the two sets. I see no path to this from subset-product spacing alone.

---

# 6. Phase-relaxed countermodel

The strongest obstruction is a phase-relaxed model that preserves genuine \(Z_\alpha\)-symbol rank.

Let \(M\ge s+3\). Consider independent torus variables \(z_p\in S^1\), and define:

\[
Z(z,\alpha)
=
\sum_{p\in\mathcal P} a_p(\alpha)z_p+\text{dual/background},
\]

\[
W(z,\alpha)
=
\sum_{|S|=s}\widetilde C_S(\alpha)\prod_{p\in S}z_p.
\]

Assume \(r_p(\alpha)\) is genuinely non-polynomial in \(\log p\), so \(Z_\alpha\) adds symbol rank.

Even then, the system

\[
Z(z,\alpha)=0,
\qquad
W(z,\alpha)=0
\]

is underdetermined on \(\mathbb T^M\) for \(M\gg1\). Generically, two complex equations impose four real conditions, leaving dimension

\[
M-4
\]

before using \(\alpha\), and still positive for the relevant active dimensions.

Thus in the relaxed torus model there is no obstruction to simultaneous vanishing of \(Z_\alpha\) and the Wronskian determinant.

By Kronecker recurrence, the one-parameter orbit

\[
z_p=p^{-im}
\]

can approximate such a torus point with recurrence cost roughly

\[
Q^{O(M)}.
\]

For \(M\asymp R\), this is

\[
Q^{O(R)}
=
\exp\left(
O\!\left(\frac{(\log Q)^2}{\log\log Q}\right)
\right).
\]

This is still too cheap.

Therefore the determinant route cannot be justified by:

\[
\text{symbol rank}
+
\text{generic torus recurrence}
+
\text{subset-product uniqueness}.
\]

It requires additional actual-zeta arithmetic.

---

# 7. One-parameter recurrence countermodel

A more realistic surrogate fixes the coefficients and uses the actual prime-log orbit.

Let \(z^\ast\in\mathbb T^M\) solve

\[
Z(z^\ast,\alpha)=0,
\qquad
W(z^\ast,\alpha)=0.
\]

If the logarithms \(\log p_1,\dots,\log p_M\) are rationally independent, then for every \(\varepsilon>0\) there exists \(m\) such that

\[
\max_p |p^{-im}-z_p^\ast|<\varepsilon.
\]

The Lipschitz constant of \(W\) is at most roughly

\[
\operatorname{Lip}(W)
\lesssim
s\sum_S|\widetilde C_S|.
\]

So to force

\[
|W(z(m),\alpha)|<Q^{-A},
\]

it suffices to approximate the torus point to accuracy

\[
\varepsilon
\lesssim
\frac{Q^{-A}}{s\sum_S|\widetilde C_S|}.
\]

If normalization keeps

\[
\sum_S|\widetilde C_S|\le Q^{O(1)}
\]

or even \(e^{O(R\log R)}=Q^{O(1)}\), then the required \(\varepsilon\) is still polynomially small in \(Q\). The recurrence cost remains

\[
Q^{O(M)}.
\]

Again, for \(M\asymp R\), recurrence is too cheap.

This does not disprove the actual theorem, because actual admissible centers are not arbitrary recurrence hits; they are tied to off-line packet geometry and the full canonical AFE. But it is a serious obstruction to a purely determinant-based proof.

---

# 8. Does \(Z_\alpha\)-incidence rescue the route?

Only if one proves a genuinely arithmetic incidence theorem.

Symbol transversality means the rows

\[
Z_\alpha,\quad q''_{\rm can},\quad \mathcal D q''_{\rm can},\dots,\mathcal D^R q''_{\rm can}
\]

are not formally redundant.

But the desired conclusion is stronger:

\[
Z_\alpha(m)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|W(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This is not a consequence of formal rank. In fact, at an exact bad point where

\[
Z_\alpha(m)=0,
\qquad
\mathcal D^j q''_{\rm can}(m)=0,
\quad 0\le j\le R,
\]

the Wronskian matrix has a zero column, so

\[
W(m,\alpha)=0.
\]

Therefore the Wronskian route is logically equivalent to proving the bad point cannot exist. The determinant is not independently protected against smallness.

So the needed theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual zeta arithmetic forbids the joint incidence }
Z_\alpha=0,\quad W\approx0.
}
\]

That is a fixed actual-zeta nonrecurrence theorem, not a Wronskian algebra theorem.

---

# 9. Positive mechanisms checked

## 9.1 Coefficient dominance

No natural dominance on thick blocks.

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{\text{not visible}}
\]

## 9.2 Sign / positivity

No fixed sign or Weil-positive structure in the subset-product determinant.

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{\text{not available}}
\]

## 9.3 Sparse block isolation

If one chooses \(M=s\), the determinant has one subset term, hence no subset cancellation. But then:

\[
W(m,\alpha)=\widetilde C_S(\alpha)e^{-im\log N_S}
\]

and lower-bounding \(W\) reduces to lower-bounding the coefficient. This is algebraically attractive.

The problem is that a single sparse minor does not automatically control the full canonical Wronskian, and it may not survive \(B_2,D_2,E_2\) or thick-block transversality requirements.

This is promising only if Agent 4 can define a legitimate filtered minor that remains local and canonical.

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{\text{possible only via filtered-minor route}}
\]

## 9.4 Subset-product spacing

Gives \(L^2\) average mass, not pointwise lower bound.

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{\text{insufficient}}
\]

## 9.5 \(Z_\alpha\)-incidence exclusion

This is the only genuinely promising mechanism, but it is exactly the hard arithmetic theorem.

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{\text{unresolved; core missing theorem}}
\]

---

# 10. Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Subset-product Wronskian nonrecurrence remains unresolved and cannot be proved from determinant structure alone.}
}
\]

More sharply:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The normalized Wronskian determinant is vulnerable to phase cancellation in realistic surrogate models.}
}
\]

Even when \(Z_\alpha\) adds genuine symbol rank, the torus model still allows

\[
Z_\alpha=0,
\qquad
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R=0,
\]

and the one-parameter prime-log orbit can plausibly recur near such points within the admissible \(Q\)-budget when the active dimension is \(O(R)\).

Therefore the determinant route is not dead, but the remaining theorem must be explicitly arithmetic:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

for the full canonical object

\[
q''_{\rm can}=B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2.
\]

Current classification:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Wronskian route: Yellow.}
}
\]

It is Green only if one proves actual-zeta incidence exclusion. It is not enough to show formal symbol rank, Cauchy--Binet nonvanishing, or average determinant mass.

## Assistant (2026-04-26 19:11:38)

I’ll handle the basis-normalization problem as Agent 3/C. The key question is whether the apparent Vandermonde loss is a basis artifact or an intrinsic sampling-volume loss.

_Thought for 1m 35s_

## Agent 3 / C — Basis-normalized Wronskian conditioning

I am executing the **Agent 3 / C** instruction: replace the monomial Wronskian by a better-conditioned basis-normalized Wronskian and determine whether the apparent \(\exp(O(R^2))\) Vandermonde loss is avoidable. The coordinator prompt identifies this as one of the current core subclaims for the normalized Wronskian route. 

---

# 1. Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The } \exp(O(R^2)) \text{ loss is avoidable only after basis-normalizing the Wronskian.}
}
\]

For the **raw monomial Wronskian**, the \(\exp(O(R^2))\) Vandermonde/capacity factor is intrinsic.

For a **basis-normalized Wronskian**, using stable Lagrange / Chebyshev / discrete-orthonormal polynomial bases on a well-spaced active block, the conditioning cost can plausibly be reduced to

\[
\boxed{
\exp(O(R\log R))=Q^{O(1)}
}
\]

at the safe depth

\[
R\asymp \frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q}.
\]

However, this does **not** solve determinant nonrecurrence. It only removes a false conditioning barrier. The remaining hard theorem is still the actual incidence/nonrecurrence lower bound

\[
Z_\alpha(m)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

---

# 2. Setup

Let the active block be

\[
\mathcal B=\{n_1,\dots,n_M\},
\]

and normalize frequencies by

\[
x_n
=
\frac{\Delta\log n-x_0}{L}
\quad\text{or}\quad
x_n
=
\frac{\lambda\log n-x_0}{L},
\qquad
x_n\in[-1,1].
\]

Let

\[
r_n(\alpha)=\frac{z_n(\alpha)}{c_n}
\]

be the \(Z_\alpha\)-to-\(q''_{\rm can}\) symbol ratio.

The dangerous monomial determinant is essentially

\[
\det
\begin{pmatrix}
r_{n_0} & r_{n_1} & \cdots & r_{n_{R+1}}\\
1       & 1       & \cdots & 1\\
x_{n_0} & x_{n_1} & \cdots & x_{n_{R+1}}\\
\vdots  & \vdots  &        & \vdots\\
x_{n_0}^{R} & x_{n_1}^{R} & \cdots & x_{n_{R+1}}^{R}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

This determinant equals a Vandermonde factor times a divided-difference residual. The Vandermonde factor can be exponentially small in \(R^2\), even at well-spaced nodes.

So the monomial basis is the wrong normalization.

---

# 3. Raw monomial loss is intrinsic

For \(d=R+1\), the polynomial evaluation determinant satisfies

\[
\det(x_i^j)_{0\le i,j\le d}
=
\prod_{0\le i<j\le d}(x_j-x_i).
\]

Even for optimal Fekete / Chebyshev-like nodes in \([-1,1]\), the maximal Vandermonde has capacity-scale size

\[
\max_{x_i\in[-1,1]}
\left|
\prod_{i<j}(x_j-x_i)
\right|
=
\exp(-c d^2+O(d\log d)).
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\left|
\det(x_i^j)
\right|^{-1}
=
\exp(O(R^2)).
\]

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No monomial-coordinate proof can avoid } \exp(O(R^2)) \text{ conditioning.}
}
\]

This is not a zeta obstruction; it is purely a bad-coordinate obstruction.

---

# 4. Chebyshev / Legendre basis improves operator norms but not raw determinant comparison

If one uses Chebyshev polynomials

\[
T_0,T_1,\dots,T_d,
\]

then

\[
\det(T_j(x_i))_{0\le i,j\le d}
=
\left(\prod_{j=1}^d 2^{j-1}\right)
\prod_{i<j}(x_j-x_i)
=
2^{d(d-1)/2}
\prod_{i<j}(x_j-x_i).
\]

For orthonormal Legendre polynomials, the leading coefficient product is similarly

\[
\exp(O(d^2)).
\]

So relative to raw monomials, the determinant transformation factor is still

\[
\exp(O(R^2)).
\]

However, the relevant point is different: the **individual basis functions** have controlled norms, and the corresponding differential operators have coefficients growing only like

\[
\exp(O(R))
\quad\text{or conservatively}\quad
\exp(O(R\log R)).
\]

Thus Chebyshev/Legendre normalization is acceptable for building a normalized Wronskian, but not for proving a lower bound for the raw monomial determinant.

---

# 5. Best basis: local Lagrange basis on a stable node set

The cleanest normalization is a **local Lagrange basis** on a well-spaced witness set.

Choose \(R+2\) active nodes

\[
S=\{x_0,\dots,x_{R+1}\}\subset[-1,1]
\]

with Chebyshev-like spacing, or at least with Lebesgue constant

\[
\Lambda_S\le \exp(O(R\log R)).
\]

For Chebyshev-like nodes one has the stronger bound

\[
\Lambda_S=O(\log R).
\]

Let

\[
\ell_0,\dots,\ell_R
\]

be the degree-\(\le R\) Lagrange basis attached to \(x_0,\dots,x_R\), so

\[
\ell_j(x_i)=\delta_{ij},
\qquad
0\le i,j\le R.
\]

Then the augmented \(Z_\alpha\)-symbol determinant is

\[
A_S
=
\det
\begin{pmatrix}
r(x_0)&r(x_1)&\cdots&r(x_R)&r(x_{R+1})\\
\ell_0(x_0)&\ell_0(x_1)&\cdots&\ell_0(x_R)&\ell_0(x_{R+1})\\
\vdots&\vdots&&\vdots&\vdots\\
\ell_R(x_0)&\ell_R(x_1)&\cdots&\ell_R(x_R)&\ell_R(x_{R+1})
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

By elementary row reduction,

\[
\boxed{
A_S
=
r(x_{R+1})
-
I_R r(x_{R+1}),
}
\]

where \(I_R r\) is the degree-\(\le R\) interpolant through \(x_0,\dots,x_R\).

So the augmented determinant is exactly an interpolation residual. There is no Vandermonde loss.

---

# 6. Residual determinant from \(L^\infty\) polynomial residual

Define

\[
\delta_\infty
=
\inf_{P\in\mathcal P_{\le R}}
\|r-P\|_{L^\infty(I)}.
\]

For any interpolation operator \(I_R\),

\[
I_R r\in \mathcal P_{\le R},
\]

hence

\[
\|r-I_R r\|_{L^\infty(I)}
\ge \delta_\infty.
\]

Therefore there exists some witness point \(x_\ast\in I\) such that

\[
|r(x_\ast)-I_Rr(x_\ast)|\ge \delta_\infty.
\]

If the active block is dense enough to contain a node \(x_{R+1}\) within mesh size \(h\) of such a witness, and if

\[
\|(r-I_Rr)'\|_\infty h
\le \frac12\delta_\infty,
\]

then

\[
\boxed{
|A_S|\ge \frac12\delta_\infty.
}
\]

So the \(Z_\alpha\)-symbol residual determinant can indeed be bounded below by the \(L^\infty\) polynomial residual, up to mesh and interpolation-stability losses.

With stable Chebyshev-like anchors, those losses are at worst

\[
\exp(O(R\log R)),
\]

and often only polynomial in \(R\).

Thus the desired implication is viable:

\[
\boxed{
\inf_{P\in\mathcal P_{\le R}}\|r-P\|_\infty\ge Q^{-C}
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|A_S|\ge Q^{-C'}
}
\]

provided the active block supplies stable nodes and sufficient sampling density.

---

# 7. Column / derivative-side normalization

The Wronskian also has a derivative-column determinant. In monomial coordinates it contributes another Vandermonde-type factor.

Use a degree-\(\le R+1\) Lagrange basis

\[
\eta_0,\dots,\eta_{R+1}
\]

on the full witness set \(S\), satisfying

\[
\eta_j(x_i)=\delta_{ij}.
\]

Then the derivative-side evaluation determinant is

\[
B_S
=
\det(\eta_j(x_i))_{0\le i,j\le R+1}
=
1.
\]

Thus the basis-normalized Wronskian coefficient for the selected subset has the schematic size

\[
\boxed{
\widetilde C_S
\sim
\left(\prod_{n\in S} c_n\right)
\bigl(r-I_Rr\bigr)(x_{R+1}),
}
\]

up to the chosen amplitude normalization and AFE weights.

Again, no Vandermonde loss remains in the normalized coefficient.

---

# 8. Operator-cost estimate

The normalized basis functions \(\ell_j,\eta_j\) must be represented as polynomials in

\[
\mathcal D=\lambda\partial_m
\quad\text{or}\quad
\mathcal D=\Delta\partial_m.
\]

For Chebyshev-like nodes, the Lagrange polynomials have controlled expansion in Chebyshev basis:

\[
\ell_j(x)
=
\sum_{k\le R} a_{jk}T_k(x),
\]

with

\[
\sum_{k\le R}|a_{jk}|
\le \operatorname{poly}(R)
\]

in the ideal Chebyshev case, and conservatively

\[
\sum_{k\le R}|a_{jk}|
\le \exp(O(R\log R))
\]

for merely well-separated active nodes.

Since Chebyshev polynomials satisfy

\[
T_k(x)=2^{k-1}x^k+\cdots,
\]

conversion back to raw derivative powers may cost

\[
\exp(O(R)).
\]

For nonoptimal but separated nodes, use the conservative bound

\[
\boxed{
\text{basis-operator norm}
\le
\exp(O(R\log R)).
}
\]

At

\[
R\asymp \frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\]

this gives

\[
\exp(O(R\log R))
=
Q^{O(1)}.
\]

Therefore the basis normalization is compatible with the canonical derivative/error budget.

---

# 9. Discrete orthonormal alternative

A more invariant version uses discrete orthonormal polynomials on the active block.

Let \(\mu\) be a probability measure on \(\mathcal B\), and let

\[
\phi_0,\dots,\phi_R
\]

be orthonormal in \(\ell^2(\mathcal B,\mu)\). Let

\[
\rho
=
r-\Pi_{\mathcal P_{\le R}}r,
\qquad
\delta_2=\|\rho\|_{\ell^2(\mu)}.
\]

Define the augmented row matrix

\[
A=
\begin{pmatrix}
\rho\\
\phi_0\\
\vdots\\
\phi_R
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Then the rows are orthogonal and

\[
AA^\ast
=
\operatorname{diag}(\delta_2^2,1,\dots,1).
\]

By Cauchy--Binet,

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{|S|=R+2}|\det A_S|^2
=
\delta_2^2.
}
\]

Hence there exists at least one subset \(S\) with

\[
|\det A_S|
\ge
\frac{\delta_2}{\sqrt{\binom{M}{R+2}}}.
\]

If \(M\le C R\), then

\[
\binom{M}{R+2}
=
\exp(O(R)),
\]

so

\[
|\det A_S|
\ge
\delta_2\exp(-O(R)).
\]

This is even better than the requested

\[
\exp(-O(R\log R)).
\]

However, this only controls **some** subset determinant. It does not by itself ensure that the same subset also has a large derivative-column determinant unless the derivative basis is chosen compatibly or the node family is well-conditioned.

So the discrete orthonormal basis is useful for exterior-norm conditioning, but the Lagrange witness basis is cleaner for producing one explicit normalized minor.

---

# 10. What remains unresolved

The basis-normalized construction solves only the conditioning problem.

It does **not** solve the subset-product cancellation problem:

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)
=
\sum_S \widetilde C_S(\alpha)e^{-im\log N_S}.
\]

Even if one coefficient satisfies

\[
|\widetilde C_S|\ge Q^{-C},
\]

the full determinant may still be small because many subset-product phases can cancel.

Therefore the remaining theorem is still:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m)=0
\Rightarrow
\left|
\sum_S \widetilde C_S(\alpha)e^{-im\log N_S}
\right|
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Basis normalization ensures that a proof of this theorem is not killed by artificial Vandermonde losses. It does not supply the arithmetic incidence exclusion.

---

# 11. Basis-normalized determinant theorem

A useful theorem statement is:

**Theorem candidate.**  
Let \(\mathcal B\) be an active canonical AFE block. Suppose there exists a stable witness set

\[
S=\{x_0,\dots,x_{R+1}\}\subset\mathcal B
\]

with interpolation stability

\[
\Lambda_S\le \exp(O(R\log R)).
\]

Suppose also that the \(Z_\alpha\)-symbol ratio satisfies

\[
\inf_{P\in\mathcal P_{\le R}}
\|r_\alpha-P\|_{L^\infty(I)}
\ge Q^{-C}
\]

and the active block is dense enough to sample a residual witness without losing more than \(Q^{-C'}\).

Then there exists a basis-normalized Wronskian minor with coefficient

\[
|\widetilde C_S(\alpha)|
\ge
Q^{-C''}
\prod_{n\in S}|c_n|,
\]

after amplitude normalization, and all row/column basis transformations have operator norm

\[
\le \exp(O(R\log R))=Q^{O(1)}.
\]

Thus the basis-normalized determinant avoids the monomial

\[
\exp(O(R^2))
\]

loss.

---

# 12. Degeneration cases

The theorem can fail in the following cases:

### 1. No stable active nodes

If the active block does not contain \(R+2\) well-spaced nodes, Lagrange interpolation may have

\[
\Lambda_S\gg \exp(O(R\log R)).
\]

Then conditioning can again become non-polynomial.

### 2. Symbol residual too small

If

\[
\inf_{P\in\mathcal P_{\le R}}\|r_\alpha-P\|_\infty
\ll Q^{-C},
\]

then no basis change can create rank. This is the \(\alpha\to0\), flat-block, low-intrinsic-variation obstruction.

### 3. Sampling too coarse

If the maximum of \(r-I_Rr\) occurs between active frequencies and the block is too sparse to sample it, the discrete determinant can be much smaller than the continuous residual.

### 4. Amplitude zeros

If \(c_n\), \(z_n(\alpha)\), or the AFE smoothing weights vanish or are too small on the witness set, the normalized determinant may be algebraically well-conditioned but canonically irrelevant.

### 5. Comparing back to raw monomials

If one insists on a raw monomial Wronskian lower bound, the \(\exp(O(R^2))\) factor returns.

---

# 13. Final classification

\[
\boxed{
\text{Basis-normalized Wronskian conditioning: Green feasible.}
}
\]

More precisely:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The } \exp(O(R^2)) \text{ Vandermonde loss is a coordinate artifact for the normalized route.}
}
\]

A stable Lagrange / Chebyshev / discrete-orthonormal normalization reduces the relevant conditioning cost to

\[
\exp(O(R\log R))=Q^{O(1)}
\]

at

\[
R\asymp \frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q}.
\]

But:

\[
\boxed{
\text{This does not prove the Wronskian lower bound.}
}
\]

It only clears the determinant-conditioning subproblem. The hard unsolved part remains the actual zeta incidence theorem:

\[
Z_\alpha(m)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]
