## Agent 3 / C — Direct incidence theorem as actual-orbit nonrecurrence

I am executing the **Agent 3 / C** instruction: attack

\[
Z_\alpha(m)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

directly, treating \(Z_\alpha\) and \(\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\) as actual fixed-coefficient exponential sums on the one-parameter zeta orbit. 

---

# 1. Packet-frequency AFE form

Work in the ordinary packet regime:

\[
\lambda Q\asymp1,
\qquad
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q}.
\]

The active packet-frequency block is

\[
\mathcal B_{\rm pf}
=
\{n:cQ\le \log n\le cQ+Q/R\},
\qquad 0<c<1/2.
\]

Thus

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n\asymp1,
\qquad
\operatorname{diam}(x_n)\asymp R^{-1}.
\]

The \(Z_\alpha\)-side AFE has the form

\[
Z_\alpha(m)
=
Z_\alpha^+(m)+Z_\alpha^-(m)+E_Z(m),
\]

where

\[
Z_\alpha^+(m)
=
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_+}
z_n^+(\alpha,m)e^{-im\log n},
\]

and

\[
Z_\alpha^-(m)
=
e^{i\Theta_Z(m)}
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_-}
z_n^-(\alpha,m)e^{im\log n}.
\]

The canonical source is

\[
q''_{\rm can}
=
B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2.
\]

The basis-normalized Wronskian expands as

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)
=
\sum_{\Omega}
C_\Omega(\alpha,m)e^{-im\Omega}
+
\text{folded dual/mixed terms}
+
\text{background/error}.
\]

In the pure main branch,

\[
\Omega=\log N_S,
\qquad
N_S=\prod_{n\in S}n,
\qquad
|S|=R+2.
\]

Mixed main/dual terms have phases such as

\[
\log N_+-\log N_-,
\]

plus gamma-phase shifts

\[
e^{ik\Theta(m)}e^{-im(\log N_+-\log N_-)}.
\]

Thus the direct incidence problem is a simultaneous small-value problem for two actual exponential-polynomial observables:

\[
Z_\alpha(m),
\qquad
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha).
\]

---

# 2. The joint small-value event

The target theorem is equivalent to excluding

\[
|Z_\alpha(m)|\le Q^{-A_Z},
\qquad
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\le Q^{-A_W}
\]

at admissible ordinary packet centers.

A direct formulation is:

\[
\boxed{
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This avoids decomposing the determinant into

\[
|\mathbf z'|,\quad |\mathbf M|,\quad \text{angle},
\]

but it does not remove the core arithmetic difficulty.

---

# 3. Effective codimension on the actual one-parameter orbit

Let the effective active prime phases be

\[
u_p(m)=p^{-im}.
\]

The one-parameter orbit is

\[
m\mapsto u(m)\in\mathbb T^d,
\]

where \(d\) is the number of independent prime phases participating in the packet-frequency blocks.

On the full torus, the simultaneous small-value set

\[
\mathcal S_\varepsilon
=
\{u: |Z_\alpha(u)|\le\varepsilon,\ |W(u)|\le\varepsilon\}
\]

has generic real codimension \(4\), because \(Z_\alpha\) and \(W\) are complex-valued.

If it is nonempty and transverse, its torus measure is heuristically

\[
\operatorname{meas}(\mathcal S_\varepsilon)
\asymp \varepsilon^4.
\]

Along a generic equidistributed one-parameter orbit, the expected hitting time is therefore

\[
\varepsilon^{-4}.
\]

Taking

\[
\varepsilon=Q^{-A}
\]

gives

\[
\tau_{\rm hit}\asymp Q^{4A}.
\]

That is too cheap to prove a universal lower bound over the natural height range.

This is worse than a prescribed-phase recurrence estimate. Hitting a fixed phase point costs roughly

\[
\varepsilon^{-d},
\]

but hitting a codimension-four small-value tube costs only

\[
\varepsilon^{-4}.
\]

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The determinant does not raise generic recurrence cost enough.}
}
\]

The actual incidence theorem cannot follow from codimension alone.

---

# 4. Does the actual orbit help?

The true orbit is not arbitrary; it is generated by

\[
m\mapsto (e^{-im\log p})_p.
\]

The logs of primes are rationally independent, so the orbit is dense in every finite prime-phase torus.

This helps adversarially, not positively: if the torus small-value set is nonempty and not extremely thin in an arithmetic sense, the orbit should approximate it.

Thus a positive theorem would need to show that the relevant small-value set is **empty or arithmetically avoided** for actual zeta coefficients.

A possible statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}
\bigl(
u(m),
\{u:Z_\alpha(u)=0,\ W(u)=0\}
\bigr)
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

for all admissible centers \(m\). But this is simply the actual-orbit incidence theorem in geometric language.

No generic Diophantine property of the prime-log orbit gives this.

---

# 5. Actual coefficient structure

The coefficients are not arbitrary:

\[
z_n^\pm,\quad c_n^\pm,\quad C_\Omega
\]

come from the AFE, Euler-product data, gamma factors, and basis-normalized determinant algebra.

The positive hope is that these exact coefficients make

\[
Z_\alpha=0
\]

incompatible with

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\approx0.
\]

However, the current structure does not exhibit:

1. positivity;
2. coefficient dominance;
3. sign-definiteness;
4. a Hermitian form;
5. a main/dual commutator lower bound;
6. a resultant with a known polynomial lower bound.

The determinant is multilinear in the \(q''_{\rm can}\)-jet rows. The scalar equation

\[
Z_\alpha^+(m)+Z_\alpha^-(m)\approx0
\]

does not visibly force a lower bound for a high-degree subset-product determinant built from \(q''_{\rm can}\).

Thus exact coefficients are necessary, but no soft exploitation of them is currently available.

---

# 6. Phase-relaxed countermodel

A phase-relaxed model preserves the coefficient geometry but relaxes the orbit.

Let

\[
u=(u_p)_p\in\mathbb T^d.
\]

Define

\[
Z_\alpha(u)
=
\sum_p z_pu_p+\text{dual/background},
\]

and

\[
W(u)
=
\sum_S C_S\prod_{p\in S}u_p
+
\text{dual/mixed/background}.
\]

Assume healthy diagnostics:

\[
\rho_{\rm rank}\ge Q^{-C},
\qquad
\kappa_{\rm basis}\le Q^C,
\qquad
\sum_S|C_S|^2\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

Then the system

\[
Z_\alpha(u)=0,
\qquad
W(u)=0
\]

is two complex equations in \(d\) phase variables. For \(d\gg4\), it is generically solvable.

This model can also maintain non-small derivative and minor norms:

\[
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A},
\qquad
|\mathbf M|\ge Q^{-A},
\]

while imposing

\[
\langle \mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle=0.
\]

Therefore, the incidence theorem fails in the relaxed coefficient-compatible torus model.

The exact missing input is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual zeta orbit and actual folded main/dual coupling forbid these relaxed solutions.}
}
\]

---

# 7. Main/dual terms do not yet raise recurrence cost

The main/dual expansion creates additional phase families:

\[
e^{-im\log N_+},
\qquad
e^{+im\log N_-},
\qquad
e^{-im\log(N_+/N_-)},
\qquad
e^{ik\Theta(m)}e^{-im(\log N_+-\log N_-)}.
\]

These increase fidelity but do not by themselves create a lower bound.

Branch separation

\[
0<c<c_1<1/2
\]

improves symbol rank and avoids saddle overlap. It does not impose a sign or coercive imaginary part.

So the exact main/dual structure currently changes the object from a generic subset-product sum into a **folded subset-product sum**, but it does not yet supply a proof of nonrecurrence.

---

# 8. Candidate theorem: actual-orbit incidence exclusion

The theorem needed is:

## Actual-orbit incidence exclusion

Let

\[
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\qquad
\lambda Q\asymp1.
\]

Let \(Z_\alpha\) and \(\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\) be the full canonical packet-frequency objects built from

\[
q''_{\rm can}=B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2.
\]

Assume:

1. packet-frequency active blocks;
2. two-branch \(Z_\alpha\)-symbol rank;
3. basis-normalized determinant conditioning;
4. AFE derivative control through order \(2R+1\);
5. exclusion of saddle/transition degeneracy.

Then for every admissible ordinary center \(m\),

\[
\boxed{
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

At a zero, this gives

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m)=0
\Rightarrow
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This theorem would close the ordinary Wronskian route.

But it remains unproved.

---

# 9. What would be enough to prove it?

A proof needs one of the following genuinely arithmetic inputs.

## 9.1 Small-value repulsion

A theorem of the form:

\[
|Z_\alpha(m)|\le Q^{-A}
\Rightarrow
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A'}.
\]

This is the desired result directly.

## 9.2 Zero-set large sieve

A theorem showing that zeros of \(Z_\alpha\) cannot lie inside the small-value set of \(W\):

\[
\sum_{m_j:Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_j,\alpha)|^2
\ge Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}.
\]

This would need an explicit-formula or argument-principle mechanism.

## 9.3 Effective resultant with polynomial lower bound

A true exponential-polynomial resultant satisfying

\[
\operatorname{Res}_{\rm exp}(Z_\alpha,W)\ne0
\]

and giving

\[
|Z_\alpha|+|W|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Formal nonzero resultant is insufficient; the lower bound must be polynomial in \(Q\).

## 9.4 Main/dual incompatibility

A theorem showing that

\[
Z_\alpha^++Z_\alpha^-=0
\]

forces some folded determinant component of \(W\) to be large.

No such invariant is currently visible.

---

# 10. No-go conclusion

The following do **not** prove the direct incidence theorem:

\[
\text{codimension},
\]

\[
\text{generic recurrence},
\]

\[
\text{symbol rank},
\]

\[
\text{basis conditioning},
\]

\[
\text{mean-square determinant mass},
\]

\[
\text{branch separation},
\]

\[
\text{formal resultant nonvanishing}.
\]

All of them leave open simultaneous smallness along the actual orbit.

The core obstruction is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{generic recurrence predicts hits unless actual-zeta arithmetic forbids them.}
}
\]

---

# 11. Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Direct incidence remains open.}
}
\]

More precisely:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The ordinary packet-frequency Wronskian route now reduces to an actual-orbit incidence theorem.}
}
\]

I do not see a proof from the currently available Wronskian, AFE, or main/dual machinery.

The next mathematical target should be stated explicitly as:

\[
\boxed{
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

for the full canonical packet-frequency objects. Without a new actual-zeta small-value repulsion / zero-set large sieve / effective resultant theorem, the incidence step remains unclosed.

## Agent 3 / C — Direct actual-orbit incidence theorem

I am executing the **Agent 3 / C** instruction: attack the full incidence theorem

\[
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A}
\]

directly, without relying on the \(\mathbf z'\), \(\mathbf M\), angle decomposition unless it helps. The latest prompt asks specifically whether the actual one-parameter zeta orbit changes the generic recurrence obstruction. 

---

# 1. Packet-frequency AFE form

Work in the ordinary packet-frequency regime:

\[
\lambda Q\asymp1,
\qquad
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\]

with active blocks

\[
\mathcal B_{\rm pf}
=
\{n:cQ\le \log n\le cQ+Q/R\},
\qquad
0<c<1/2.
\]

Then

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n\asymp1,
\qquad
\operatorname{diam}(x_n)\asymp R^{-1}.
\]

The relevant AFE forms are

\[
Z_\alpha(m)
=
Z_\alpha^+(m)+Z_\alpha^-(m)+E_Z(m),
\]

where

\[
Z_\alpha^+(m)
=
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_+}
z_n^+(\alpha,m)e^{-im\log n},
\]

and

\[
Z_\alpha^-(m)
=
e^{i\Theta_Z(m)}
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_-}
z_n^-(\alpha,m)e^{im\log n}.
\]

The canonical source curvature is

\[
q''_{\rm can}
=
B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2.
\]

The basis-normalized Wronskian expands schematically as

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)
=
\sum_{\Omega}
C_\Omega(\alpha,m)e^{-im\Omega}
+
\text{folded dual/mixed terms}
+
\text{background/error}.
\]

For the pure-main determinant contribution,

\[
\Omega=\log N_S,
\qquad
N_S=\prod_{n\in S}n,
\qquad
|S|=R+2.
\]

Mixed main/dual terms have frequencies of the form

\[
\log N_+-\log N_-,
\]

with additional gamma-phase factors

\[
e^{ik\Theta(m)}.
\]

Thus the direct incidence theorem is a simultaneous small-value exclusion for two actual exponential-polynomial observables on the same \(m\)-orbit.

---

# 2. Small-value event

The forbidden event is

\[
|Z_\alpha(m)|\le Q^{-A_Z},
\qquad
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\le Q^{-A_W}.
\]

Equivalently, one wants

\[
\boxed{
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

at every admissible ordinary packet center.

At an exact \(Z_\alpha\)-zero this becomes

\[
\boxed{
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is the cleanest direct formulation. It avoids proving the three separate estimates

\[
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A},
\qquad
|\mathbf M|\ge Q^{-A},
\qquad
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge Q^{-A}|\mathbf z'||\mathbf M|,
\]

but it does not make the arithmetic easier.

---

# 3. Actual one-parameter orbit and codimension

Let the active phases be

\[
u_p(m)=e^{-im\log p}.
\]

For a finite prime set \(\mathcal P\), the actual orbit is

\[
m\mapsto u(m)\in \mathbb T^{|\mathcal P|}.
\]

On the full phase torus, the set

\[
\mathcal S_\varepsilon
=
\{u: |Z_\alpha(u)|\le\varepsilon,\ |W(u)|\le\varepsilon\}
\]

has generic real codimension \(4\), since both \(Z_\alpha\) and \(W=\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\) are complex-valued.

If \(\mathcal S_\varepsilon\) is nonempty and transverse, then heuristically

\[
\operatorname{meas}(\mathcal S_\varepsilon)\asymp \varepsilon^4.
\]

A generic equidistributed one-parameter orbit should hit such a set after time roughly

\[
\varepsilon^{-4}.
\]

Taking

\[
\varepsilon=Q^{-A}
\]

gives recurrence cost

\[
Q^{O(1)}.
\]

That is far too cheap to support a universal lower bound over the natural height range. So the actual one-parameter nature of the orbit does **not** rescue the theorem by codimension alone.

The important distinction is:

\[
\text{hitting a prescribed phase point costs }\varepsilon^{-d},
\]

but

\[
\text{hitting a codimension-four small-value tube costs }\varepsilon^{-4}.
\]

The incidence target is the second type.

---

# 4. Exact coefficients do not yet give repulsion

The exact coefficients are special:

\[
z_n^\pm,\qquad c_n^\pm,\qquad C_\Omega,
\]

come from AFE weights, gamma factors, \(B_2\), the dual branch, and basis-normalized determinant algebra.

However, I see no current mechanism giving

\[
Z_\alpha(m)\approx0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)\not\approx0.
\]

Specifically, there is no visible:

\[
\text{positivity},
\quad
\text{dominant term},
\quad
\text{sign-definite commutator},
\quad
\text{Hermitian coercivity},
\quad
\text{main/dual phase obstruction}.
\]

The scalar equation

\[
Z_\alpha^+(m)+Z_\alpha^-(m)+E_Z(m)\approx0
\]

imposes one complex relation among packet-frequency sums. It does not visibly force a high-degree subset-product determinant built from \(q''_{\rm can}\)-jets to be large.

Thus exact coefficients are necessary for a proof, but no exploitable soft structure has emerged.

---

# 5. Resultant route

One could try to define an exponential-polynomial resultant for

\[
f(m)=Z_\alpha(m),
\qquad
g(m)=\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha).
\]

The desired lower bound would be

\[
|f(m)|+|g(m)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

A formal nonzero resultant would only show that \(f\) and \(g\) do not share an identical factor or a structurally forced common zero. It would **not** exclude approximate common zeros along a long orbit.

An effective resultant would need all of the following:

1. normalized coefficient heights for \(f\) and \(g\);
2. effective degree/frequency diameter control;
3. lower bound for the resultant itself;
4. conversion from nonzero resultant to pointwise separation.

For the Wronskian determinant, the effective frequency family contains subset-products

\[
N_S=\prod_{n\in S}n,
\]

and mixed main/dual ratios

\[
N_+/N_-.
\]

Even with basis normalization, the number of frequencies is large, and a resultant lower bound would itself be a nontrivial actual-orbit arithmetic theorem.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{resultant language may repackage the incidence theorem, but does not currently simplify it.}
}
\]

---

# 6. Phase-relaxed countermodel

A phase-relaxed model keeps coefficient geometry but removes actual-orbit constraints.

Let

\[
u=(u_p)_{p\in\mathcal P}\in\mathbb T^d.
\]

Define

\[
Z_\alpha(u)
=
\sum_p z_pu_p+\text{dual/background},
\]

and

\[
W(u)
=
\sum_S C_S\prod_{p\in S}u_p
+
\text{dual/mixed/background}.
\]

Assume healthy diagnostics:

\[
\rho_{\rm rank}\ge Q^{-C},
\]

\[
\kappa_{\rm basis}\le Q^C,
\]

\[
\sum_S|C_S|^2\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

Then the system

\[
Z_\alpha(u)=0,
\qquad
W(u)=0
\]

is two complex equations in \(d\) phase variables. For \(d\gg4\), it is generically solvable.

Moreover, the healthy side conditions

\[
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A},
\qquad
|\mathbf M|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

do not prevent

\[
\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle=0.
\]

Thus the incidence theorem fails in the phase-relaxed model even with healthy symbol rank, basis conditioning, and determinant coefficient mass.

The missing ingredient is precisely:

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual zeta orbit and actual folded main/dual arithmetic forbid the relaxed common-zero pattern.}
}
\]

---

# 7. One-parameter recurrence remains too cheap

The actual orbit is dense in the finite prime-phase torus because the prime logarithms are rationally independent.

If the small-value set \(\mathcal S_\varepsilon\) is nonempty and has volume \(\asymp\varepsilon^4\), generic orbit recurrence predicts hits on scale

\[
\varepsilon^{-4}.
\]

For

\[
\varepsilon=Q^{-A},
\]

this is

\[
Q^{O(1)}.
\]

Therefore the actual orbit must have a **special avoidance property** for this particular set. Generic orbit structure does not give it.

This is the main no-go point:

\[
\boxed{
\text{codimension-four recurrence is too cheap.}
}
\]

So the direct incidence theorem needs a genuinely arithmetic repulsion statement, not just Wronskian rank.

---

# 8. Candidate theorem

The strongest useful theorem statement is:

## Actual-orbit packet-frequency incidence theorem

Let

\[
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\qquad
\lambda Q\asymp1.
\]

Let \(Z_\alpha\) and \(\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\) be the full canonical packet-frequency objects built from

\[
q''_{\rm can}=B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2.
\]

Assume:

1. packet-frequency active blocks;
2. two-branch symbol rank;
3. basis-normalized determinant conditioning;
4. high-derivative AFE control;
5. exclusion of saddle/transition degeneracy.

Then for every admissible ordinary center,

\[
\boxed{
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This theorem would close the ordinary Wronskian route.

But no proof is currently visible from the available ingredients.

---

# 9. What proof would need

A proof must supply at least one of:

### 9.1 Actual small-value repulsion

\[
|Z_\alpha(m)|\le Q^{-A}
\Rightarrow
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A'}.
\]

### 9.2 Zero-set large sieve

\[
\sum_{m_j:Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_j,\alpha)|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}.
\]

### 9.3 Effective exponential-polynomial resultant

A true resultant with a polynomial quantitative separation:

\[
|Z_\alpha|+|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R|
\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

### 9.4 Folded main/dual obstruction

A theorem showing

\[
Z_\alpha^++Z_\alpha^-=0
\]

forces some determinant component of \(\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\) to be large.

At present, none of these has been derived.

---

# 10. Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Direct actual-orbit incidence remains open.}
}
\]

More sharply:

\[
\boxed{
\text{generic recurrence remains too cheap unless actual-zeta arithmetic gives special repulsion.}
}
\]

So the ordinary packet-frequency Wronskian route is structurally sound, but it now depends on a new arithmetic theorem:

\[
\boxed{
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

for the full canonical packet-frequency objects. Current Wronskian algebra, basis normalization, symbol rank, and main/dual bookkeeping do not prove it.

## Agent 3 / C — Direct actual-orbit small-value repulsion

I am executing the **Agent 3 / C** instruction: attack the direct incidence theorem

\[
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A},
\]

looking specifically for **actual-zeta small-value repulsion**, not generic transversality. The prompt frames this as the remaining direct-incidence lane after the packet-frequency Wronskian stress tests produced no Red candidates. 

---

# 1. Packet-frequency AFE forms

Work in the ordinary packet-frequency regime:

\[
\lambda Q\asymp1,
\qquad
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q}.
\]

Use blocks

\[
\mathcal B_{\rm pf}
=
\{n:cQ\le \log n\le cQ+Q/R\},
\qquad 0<c<1/2.
\]

Then

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n\asymp1,
\qquad
\operatorname{diam}(x_n)\asymp R^{-1}.
\]

The \(Z_\alpha\)-object has canonical AFE form

\[
Z_\alpha(m)
=
Z_\alpha^+(m)+Z_\alpha^-(m)+E_Z(m),
\]

with

\[
Z_\alpha^+(m)
=
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_+}
z_n^+(\alpha,m)e^{-im\log n},
\]

and folded dual contribution

\[
Z_\alpha^-(m)
=
e^{i\Theta_Z(m)}
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_-}
z_n^-(\alpha,m)e^{im\log n}.
\]

The source curvature is

\[
q''_{\rm can}=B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2.
\]

The basis-normalized Wronskian expands as a higher-degree exponential polynomial:

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)
=
\sum_{\Omega}
C_\Omega(\alpha,m)e^{-im\Omega}
+
\text{folded dual/mixed phases}
+
\text{background/error}.

In the pure main branch,

\[
\Omega=\log N_S,
\qquad
N_S=\prod_{n\in S}n,
\qquad

\]

Mixed terms involve phases of the type

\[
\log N_+-\log N_-,
\]

with factors such as

\[
e^{ik\Theta(m)}.
\]

Thus \(Z_\alpha\) is a linear AFE exponential sum, while \(\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\) is a subset-product determinant exponential sum built from the same actual orbit.

---

# 2. Small-value set

Define

\[
\mathcal S_\varepsilon
=
\left\{
m:
|Z_\alpha(m)|\le \varepsilon,\quad
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\le \varepsilon
\right\}.
\]

The desired theorem is

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal S_{Q^{-A}}=\varnothing
}
\]

inside the admissible ordinary packet-center set.

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

At an exact \(Z_\alpha\)-zero this gives

\[
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

---

# 3. Generic recurrence remains too cheap

Let

\[
u_p(m)=p^{-im}
\]

be the active prime phases. On the finite phase torus, \(Z_\alpha\) and \(\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\) are two complex-valued functions.

If the common small-value set is nonempty and transverse, then on the relaxed torus

\[
\{|Z_\alpha|\le \varepsilon,\ |\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R|\le \varepsilon\}
\]

has real codimension \(4\), hence expected measure

\[
\asymp \varepsilon^4.
\]

A generic one-parameter orbit should hit such a tube after time

\[
\varepsilon^{-4}.
\]

For

\[
\varepsilon=Q^{-A},
\]

this is only

\[
Q^{O(1)}.
\]

So the actual one-parameter nature of the orbit does **not** by itself make the event rare enough. The determinant adds one complex observable, but that still does not raise recurrence cost beyond the available height scale.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{codimension-four recurrence is too cheap.}
}
\]

Any proof must use special actual-zeta structure, not generic orbit recurrence.

---

# 4. Could exact coefficients make the common-small set empty?

This is the only plausible direct route.

One would need a theorem saying the exact AFE coefficients force

\[
Z_\alpha(u)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(u,\alpha)\ne 0
\]

with polynomial separation.

But the current coefficient structure does not show a visible coercive mechanism.

Specifically, I see no available:

\[
\text{positive form},
\qquad
\text{dominant term},
\qquad
\text{sign-definite main/dual commutator},
\qquad
\text{Hermitian Gram identity},
\qquad
\text{known zero-repulsion identity}.
\]

The scalar equation

\[
Z_\alpha^+(m)+Z_\alpha^-(m)+E_Z(m)\approx0
\]

imposes one complex relation among linear AFE sums. The determinant

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R
\]

is a high-degree subset-product object in \(q''_{\rm can}\)-jet rows. There is currently no algebraic reason that the first relation forces the second object to be large.

So the exact coefficients may be the right place to look, but no current mechanism proves emptiness or avoidance of \(\mathcal S_\varepsilon\).

---

# 5. Resultant comparison

A formal exponential-polynomial resultant for

\[
f(m)=Z_\alpha(m),
\qquad
g(m)=\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)
\]

would aim to prove

\[
|f(m)|+|g(m)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

However, a nonzero resultant would generally prove only that \(f\) and \(g\) have no **identically forced** common factor or exact common zero in an algebraic closure.

It would not by itself exclude approximate common zeros along a long real orbit.

To make this route work, one would need:

1. effective coefficient-height control after basis normalization;
2. effective frequency-degree control for subset-product phases;
3. a polynomial lower bound for the exponential resultant;
4. a conversion from resultant lower bound to real-orbit separation.

The fourth point is essentially the incidence theorem itself. Thus the resultant language is not useless, but it repackages the same missing arithmetic lower bound.

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{
\text{resultant formulation is Yellow; it does not currently simplify the problem.}
}
\]

---

# 6. Strongest phase-relaxed countermodel

Let \(u=(u_p)\in\mathbb T^d\) be independent phases.

Use the exact-symbol surrogate:

\[
Z_\alpha(u)
=
\sum_p z_pu_p+\text{dual/background},
\]

\[
W(u)
=
\sum_S C_S\prod_{p\in S}u_p
+
\text{dual/mixed/background}.
\]

Assume healthy diagnostics:

\[
\rho_{\rm rank}\ge Q^{-C},
\qquad
\kappa_{\rm basis}\le Q^C,
\qquad
\sum_S|C_S|^2\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

Then

\[
Z_\alpha(u)=0,
\qquad
W(u)=0
\]

is two complex equations in \(d\) phases. For \(d\gg4\), the system is generically solvable.

This remains true even when the relaxed model preserves:

\[
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A},
\qquad
|\mathbf M|\ge Q^{-A},
\]

because one can still impose

\[
\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle=0.
\]

So the relaxed model shows:

\[
\boxed{
\text{rank, conditioning, and coefficient mass do not imply incidence.}
}
\]

The only missing ingredient in the model is exact actual-zeta orbit arithmetic.

---

# 7. Actual-orbit avoidance theorem needed

The needed theorem can be stated geometrically.

Let

\[
\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}
=
\{u\in\mathbb T^d:
Z_\alpha(u)=0,\ W(u)=0\}.
\]

Then the required result is a polynomial avoidance estimate:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}\bigl(u(m),\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}\bigr)
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

for all admissible ordinary centers \(m\), where

\[
u(m)=(p^{-im})_p.
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
|Z_\alpha(m)|+|W(m)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is stronger than Kronecker independence. It is an actual-orbit nonrecurrence theorem for a specific zeta-defined bad variety.

---

# 8. Possible positive theorem, conditional form

A direct theorem would look like this.

## Actual-zeta small-value repulsion theorem

Assume ordinary packet scale

\[
\lambda Q\asymp1,
\]

packet-frequency active blocks

\[
cQ\le \log n\le cQ+Q/R,
\qquad 0<c<c_1<1/2,
\]

safe depth

\[
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\]

basis-normalized Wronskians, exact \(B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2\), and high-derivative AFE control.

Then for admissible ordinary packet centers,

\[
\boxed{
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This would close the ordinary Wronskian route.

But the proof would require a new arithmetic input: actual zeta must avoid the common-small set. None of the structural Wronskian ingredients proves that avoidance.

---

# 9. What would count as progress?

A proof needs one of the following.

## 9.1 Actual small-value repulsion

\[
|Z_\alpha(m)|\le Q^{-A}
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A'}.
\]

## 9.2 Zero-set large sieve

\[
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_j,\alpha)|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}.
\]

## 9.3 Effective exponential resultant

A genuinely quantitative resultant giving

\[
|Z_\alpha|+|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R|
\ge Q^{-A},
\]

not merely non-identical vanishing.

## 9.4 Main/dual folded obstruction

A concrete invariant showing that

\[
Z_\alpha^++Z_\alpha^-=0
\]

forces at least one folded determinant component to be large.

No such invariant is currently identified.

---

# 10. Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{No direct actual-zeta small-value repulsion theorem is currently visible.}
}
\]

More sharply:

\[
\boxed{
\text{generic recurrence remains too cheap unless actual-zeta arithmetic gives special avoidance.}
}
\]

The incidence theorem remains the core open arithmetic statement:

\[
\boxed{
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The packet-frequency Wronskian framework is structurally coherent and numerically unbroken, but this final step requires new actual-zeta nonrecurrence/repulsion input beyond the current Wronskian algebra.
## User (2026-04-27 03:46:55)

Do the agent instruction in the message as per your agent number.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 03:47:02)

## Agent 3 / C — Coarse \(L^2\) packet non-cancellation on \(a\asymp R\) windows

I am executing the **Agent 3 / C** task: test whether the local \(Z_\alpha\)-amplitude theorem can be attacked through a coarse \(L^2\) estimate on a polylog-safe window, especially \(a\asymp R\). The prompt identifies this as one path to proving the missing local amplitude / zero-crowding input for \(|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}\). 

---

# 1. Packet-frequency \(L^2\) setup

Work with

\[
F(t)=Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t),
\qquad
F(0)\approx0,
\qquad
\lambda Q\asymp1.
\]

On one packet-frequency branch,

\[
F(t)
=
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B}
b_n e^{-itx_n}
+
\text{dual/background/error},
\]

where

\[
b_n=z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n},
\]

and

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n.
\]

For the ordinary block

\[
\mathcal B_{\rm pf}
=
\{n:cQ\le \log n\le cQ+Q/R\},
\]

we have

\[
x_n\asymp1,
\qquad
\operatorname{diam}\{x_n\}\asymp R^{-1}.
\]

The local \(L^2\) mass is

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2\,dt
=
\sum_{n,n'\in\mathcal B}
b_n\overline{b_{n'}}
K_a(x_n-x_{n'})
+
\text{dual/background/error},
\]

with kernel

\[
K_a(\xi)
=
\int_{-a}^{a}e^{-it\xi}\,dt
=
2\frac{\sin(a\xi)}{\xi}.
\]

Equivalently,

\[
K_a(0)=2a.
\]

---

# 2. Why \(a=O(1)\) fails

If

\[
a=O(1),
\]

then for all packet nodes,

\[
|a(x_n-x_{n'})|
\lesssim a R^{-1}
\ll1.
\]

So

\[
K_a(x_n-x_{n'})
=
2a+O(a^3|x_n-x_{n'}|^2).
\]

Therefore

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2\,dt
\approx
2a
\left|
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B}b_n
\right|^2.
\]

But \(F(0)\approx0\) is precisely

\[
\sum_n b_n+\text{dual/background}\approx0.
\]

Thus a one-packet window does **not** expose the coefficient mass. It sees essentially the same coherent packet sum that already vanishes at the zero.

So:

\[
\boxed{
a=O(1)\text{ cannot prove local amplitude from diagonal mass.}
}
\]

---

# 3. The \(a\asymp R\) window

Take

\[
a\asymp R.
\]

Then

\[
a\,\operatorname{diam}\{x_n\}
\asymp1.
\]

Now \(K_a(x_n-x_{n'})\) starts to resolve packet-frequency differences across the whole block.

For \(x_n-x_{n'}\asymp R^{-1}\),

\[
K_a(x_n-x_{n'})
\asymp
R\cdot \frac{\sin O(1)}{O(1)}.
\]

This is still not diagonal in the literal sense; the kernel has width

\[
|\xi|\lesssim a^{-1}\asymp R^{-1},
\]

which is comparable to the entire packet width. Thus \(a\asymp R\) resolves **coarse shape**, not individual frequencies.

The integral is better understood after rescaling.

Let

\[
x_n=x_0+\frac{y_n}{R},
\qquad y_n\in[0,O(1)].
\]

Then

\[
F(t)
=
e^{-itx_0}
\sum_n b_n e^{-i(t/R)y_n}.
\]

Put

\[
t=Ru,
\qquad |u|\le A_0.
\]

Then

\[
\int_{-R A_0}^{R A_0}|F(t)|^2dt
=
R
\int_{-A_0}^{A_0}
\left|
\sum_n b_n e^{-iu y_n}
\right|^2du.
\]

So the \(a\asymp R\) question is equivalent to:

\[
\boxed{
\int_{-A_0}^{A_0}
\left|
\sum_n b_n e^{-iu y_n}
\right|^2du
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is a finite-band Fourier non-cancellation statement for the rescaled packet.

---

# 4. Coarse sub-block decomposition

Partition the packet into \(L\) sub-blocks in \(y\)-space:

\[
I_r=[r/L,(r+1)/L],
\qquad 0\le r<L.
\]

Define sub-block sums

\[
B_r=\sum_{y_n\in I_r}b_n.
\]

For \(|u|\le A_0\), approximate

\[
\sum_n b_n e^{-iu y_n}
\approx
\sum_{r=0}^{L-1} B_r e^{-iu y_r}.
\]

Then

\[
\int_{-A_0}^{A_0}
\left|
\sum_n b_n e^{-iu y_n}
\right|^2du
\]

is controlled by the finite Gram matrix

\[
\sum_{r,s}B_r\overline{B_s}
\int_{-A_0}^{A_0}e^{-iu(y_r-y_s)}du.
\]

For fixed \(A_0\), this Gram matrix is positive definite only on a fixed-dimensional subspace. If \(L\) is large, it has small eigenvalues.

Therefore the coarse \(L^2\) lower bound requires a **coarse anti-design theorem**:

\[
\boxed{
(B_0,\dots,B_{L-1})
\text{ cannot lie nearly in the low-eigenvalue subspace of this Gram matrix.}
}
\]

This is not automatic from coefficient mass.

---

# 5. What coefficient mass gives

Coefficient mass gives

\[
\sum_n |b_n|^2\ge Q^{-C}
\]

after natural normalization.

But on an \(a\asymp R\) window, the kernel does not diagonalize individual \(n\). It only sees coarse packet sums.

A deterministic cancellation example:

Choose coefficients so that

\[
\sum_n b_n e^{-iu y_n}
\]

is very small on \([-A_0,A_0]\), despite

\[
\sum_n|b_n|^2=1.
\]

This is possible whenever the number of available coefficients is much larger than the effective time-band dimension. It is the standard prolate / time-band concentration obstruction.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_n|b_n|^2\text{ alone does not imply the }a\asymp R\text{ local }L^2\text{ bound.}
}
\]

One needs actual coefficient structure from \(Z_\alpha\), not merely mass.

---

# 6. Relaxed countermodel

In the relaxed model, let the phases \(b_n\) be adjustable with fixed magnitudes.

Since the packet frequencies \(y_n\) lie in a bounded interval, and the observation window \(|u|\le A_0\) has finite bandwidth, one can choose many coefficients \(b_n\) so that

\[
\sum_n b_n e^{-iu y_n}
\]

has \(R+1\) or more vanishing Taylor coefficients at \(u=0\), or is small in \(L^2[-A_0,A_0]\).

This is the same finite packet quadrature phenomenon in Fourier form.

For example, impose moment cancellations

\[
\sum_n b_n y_n^k=0,
\qquad 0\le k\le R.
\]

Then

\[
\sum_n b_n e^{-iu y_n}
=
O(|u|^{R+1})
\]

near \(u=0\). With suitable weighted quadrature, it can be small on a fixed interval.

Therefore the coarse \(L^2\) theorem fails in a phase-relaxed model.

The actual theorem would need to say:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the actual }Z_\alpha\text{ AFE coefficients cannot realize this coarse packet quadrature.}
}
\]

That is an actual prime-log non-cancellation theorem.

---

# 7. Does \(a\asymp(\log Q)^C\) help?

Let

\[
a=(\log Q)^C.
\]

The packet width is still

\[
\operatorname{diam}x\asymp R^{-1}
\asymp\frac{\log\log Q}{\log Q}.
\]

Then

\[
a\,\operatorname{diam}x
\asymp
(\log Q)^{C-1}\log\log Q.
\]

If \(C>1\), the enlarged window begins to over-resolve the original packet. In that case, the kernel width

\[
a^{-1}
\]

is smaller than the packet width, and a more diagonal estimate becomes plausible after partitioning into about

\[
L\asymp a/R
\]

resolvable frequency cells.

However, the analytic derivative reduction permits \(a\le(\log Q)^C\), but the packet-frequency AFE on such a long normalized window requires controlling \(Z_\alpha\) over physical length

\[
a\lambda
\asymp \frac{(\log Q)^C}{Q}.
\]

This is still short, but no longer a single local packet.

With \(C>1\), an \(L^2\) lower bound may follow from coefficient mass **if** the effective frequency grid has enough separation and if the phases do not cancel within each \(a^{-1}\)-cell.

The remaining obstruction is then intra-cell cancellation:

\[
B_r=\sum_{x_n\in \text{cell }r} b_n.
\]

One needs

\[
\sum_r |B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This is weaker than full coefficient non-cancellation but still not automatic. It is again an actual prime-log block-sum theorem.

---

# 8. Candidate theorem

A useful conditional theorem is:

## Coarse packet \(L^2\) non-cancellation theorem

Let

\[
F(t)=\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_{\rm pf}}b_n e^{-itx_n}
+\text{dual/background/error}
\]

with

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n,
\qquad
\operatorname{diam}x_n\asymp R^{-1}.
\]

Let \(a\in[R,(\log Q)^C]\), and partition the packet into cells of width \(a^{-1}\). Define

\[
B_r=\sum_{x_n\in I_r}b_n.
\]

Assume

\[
\sum_r |B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A_0}.
\]

Then

\[
\boxed{
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2dt\ge Q^{-A_1}
}
\]

after controlling dual/background/error terms.

Consequently,

\[
\sup_{|t|\le a}|F(t)|\ge Q^{-A_2}.
\]

This gives the local amplitude input and hence

\[
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

The missing subtheorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_r |B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A_0}
}
\]

for actual \(Z_\alpha\) AFE coefficients at admissible \(m_0\).

---

# 9. Can actual one-parameter phase constraints prove the cell bound?

The coefficients are

\[
b_n=z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}.
\]

The bad event

\[
\sum_r |B_r|^2\ll Q^{-A}
\]

means that in every frequency cell,

\[
\sum_{n\in I_r} z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}
\]

is small.

This is a family of short Dirichlet-polynomial cancellations over packet-frequency cells.

A recurrence heuristic suggests these cancellations are not impossible generically. There are many phase degrees of freedom. But the coefficients are fixed actual AFE coefficients, so one could hope for a prime-log large-sieve lower bound.

The needed theorem resembles:

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual prime phases cannot cancel all packet-frequency cell sums near a }Z_\alpha\text{-zero.}
}
\]

I do not see a proof from current inputs.

---

# 10. Relation to \(Z_\alpha(m_0)=0\)

At the zero,

\[
F(0)=\sum_n b_n+\text{dual/background}=0.
\]

This only kills the total packet sum.

The \(a\asymp R\) or larger \(L^2\) method attempts to prove that not all coarse Fourier modes vanish:

\[
\sum_n b_n y_n^k
\not\approx0
\quad\text{for some }1\le k\le R.
\]

But this is exactly the direct \(Z_\alpha\)-jet nonrecurrence theorem:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\max_{1\le k\le R+1}|\mathcal D^k Z_\alpha(m_0)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

So the \(L^2\) route and the jet route are essentially equivalent after Fourier expansion.

The \(L^2\) formulation is useful because it localizes the obstruction to coarse packet-cell sums, but it does not avoid the arithmetic theorem.

---

# 11. Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{A coarse }L^2\text{ theorem at }a\asymp R\text{ is not automatic from coefficient mass.}
}
\]

What works:

\[
a=O(1)
\]

fails because the packet is fully coherent and sees only \(F(0)\).

\[
a\asymp R
\]

resolves the packet at coarse scale and is the first plausible window.

\[
a=(\log Q)^C,\ C>1
\]

gives more resolution and may reduce the problem to non-cancellation of cell sums.

But in every case the missing input is an actual prime-log non-cancellation theorem for the \(Z_\alpha\) AFE coefficients near a zero.

So the best result is conditional:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell-sum non-cancellation}
\Rightarrow
\text{polylog-local }L^2\text{ amplitude}
\Rightarrow
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The unconditional proof still requires new actual-zeta arithmetic: either microscopic zero-crowding/local amplitude, or direct jet nonrecurrence.

## Agent 3 / C — Zero-set large sieve for \(\mathbf M\), kernel estimates

I am executing the **Agent 3 / C** instruction: push the zero-set large sieve for the cofactor/minor vector

\[
\mathbf M(m)=\sum_{|S|=R+1}\mathbf A_S e^{-im\Omega_S}+\cdots,
\qquad
\Omega_S=\log N_S,
\]

sampled on zeros of \(Z_\alpha\). The assigned target is the averaged lower bound

\[
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\ Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
\]

The current prompt frames this as one of the remaining actual-zeta arithmetic inputs after packet-frequency geometry, symbol rank, and determinant stress tests have been cleaned up. 

---

# 1. Sampling kernel and quadratic form

Let

\[
\mathcal Z_J=\{m_j\in J: Z_\alpha(m_j)=0\},
\]

and let \(w_J\) be a smooth nonnegative window on \(J\). Define

\[
K_J(\xi)
=
\sum_{m_j\in\mathcal Z_J}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j\xi}.
\]

For

\[
\mathbf M(m)
=
\sum_S \mathbf A_S e^{-im\Omega_S}
+
\text{dual/background/error},
\]

the sampled quadratic form is

\[
\sum_{m_j\in\mathcal Z_J}
w_J(m_j)|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
=
\sum_{S,T}
\langle \mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
+
\text{dual/mixed/background/error}.
\]

The diagonal term is

\[
K_J(0)\sum_S|\mathbf A_S|^2
=
\left(\sum_{m_j\in\mathcal Z_J}w_J(m_j)\right)
\sum_S|\mathbf A_S|^2.
\]

Since coefficient/exterior mass is already Green-feasible,

\[
\sum_S|\mathbf A_S|^2\ge Q^{-C},
\]

the desired zero-set large sieve follows if the off-diagonal operator is small relative to the diagonal:

\[
\boxed{
\left|
\sum_{S\ne T}
\langle \mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
\right|
\le
(1-Q^{-C})
K_J(0)\sum_S|\mathbf A_S|^2.
}
\]

Equivalently, define the Gram matrix

\[
\mathcal K_{S,T}
=
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T).
\]

We need a lower spectral bound:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{S,T}
\langle \mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle
\mathcal K_{S,T}
\ge
Q^{-A}K_J(0)\sum_S|\mathbf A_S|^2.
}
\]

This is the exact zero-set large sieve kernel problem.

---

# 2. Required kernel estimate

A sufficient estimate is:

\[
K_J(0)\asymp N_{Z_\alpha}(J),
\]

and for \(\xi\ne0\),

\[
\boxed{
|K_J(\xi)|
\le
K_J(0)\,\mathcal E(\xi),
}
\]

where the matrix with entries

\[
\mathcal E(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
\]

has operator norm at most \(1-Q^{-C}\) on the coefficient vectors \(\mathbf A_S\).

A crude sufficient condition would be

\[
|K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)|
\le
Q^{-B}K_J(0)
\qquad
(S\ne T),
\]

but this is likely too strong because the number of subset terms is enormous. A more realistic condition is an operator large-sieve bound:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{S,T}
\langle \mathbf B_S,\mathbf B_T\rangle
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
\ge
Q^{-A}K_J(0)\sum_S|\mathbf B_S|^2
}
\]

for all coefficient families \(\mathbf B_S\) supported on the packet-frequency subset-product set.

This is the clean theorem form.

---

# 3. Prime-resonance cases

For pure-main subset frequencies,

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S,
\qquad
N_S=\prod_{n\in S}n.
\]

Thus

\[
\Omega_S-\Omega_T
=
\log\frac{N_S}{N_T}.
\]

If the active frequencies are primes, then \(N_S=N_T\) if and only if \(S=T\). Therefore exact resonances are absent for distinct subsets.

But near-resonances can occur when

\[
\frac{N_S}{N_T}
\]

is close to \(1\), i.e.

\[
|\log(N_S/N_T)|\ll1.
\]

In packet-frequency blocks,

\[
\log n\in[cQ,cQ+Q/R].
\]

For \(|S|=|T|=R+1\), the total subset log-frequency lies in an interval of width

\[
(R+1)\frac{Q}{R}\asymp Q.
\]

The spacing between possible \(\log N_S\) can be very small if composites are allowed, because many different subset-products can lie close. If we restrict to primes, exact equality is impossible, but the distribution of subset sums of \(\log p\) still creates many small gaps.

So there are three resonance classes.

## 3.1 Exact diagonal

\[
S=T.
\]

This is the desired positive diagonal.

## 3.2 Multiplicative collision

\[
N_S=N_T,\qquad S\ne T.
\]

This is impossible for prime-only blocks but possible for composite \(n\)-blocks unless one tracks prime factorizations. For large-sieve cleanliness, the active block should be prime-supported or squarefree-factorization deduplicated.

## 3.3 Near-resonance

\[
0<|\log(N_S/N_T)|\le |J|^{-1}.
\]

These terms behave almost diagonally on \(J\). They are dangerous because

\[
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)\approx K_J(0).
\]

Thus the kernel theorem needs either:

1. sparse near-resonances;
2. cancellation in \(\langle \mathbf A_S,\mathbf A_T\rangle\);
3. a longer \(J\);
4. or an arithmetic zero-set kernel bound stronger than interval Fourier resolution.

---

# 4. Interval length needed from frequency spacing

For continuous sampling over \(J\),

\[
\int_J e^{-im(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)}\,dm
\]

is small when

\[
|J|\cdot|\Omega_S-\Omega_T|\gg1.
\]

The zero-set kernel cannot be better than this unless the zeros themselves add cancellation.

Therefore the large sieve needs

\[
|J|
\gg
\frac1{\min_{S\ne T}|\Omega_S-\Omega_T|}
\]

for naive diagonalization. But the minimum subset-sum spacing can be far too small.

So a direct diagonalization on all subset-products is not realistic.

A more plausible large-sieve theorem must be **spectral/operator averaged**, not pairwise diagonal. It should exploit that near-resonances are sparse or structured, so the full off-diagonal operator norm is controlled even if individual near-resonances occur.

---

# 5. Candidate zero-set large-sieve theorem

A useful theorem statement is:

## Zero-set subset-product large sieve

Let

\[
\mathcal F=\{\Omega_S=\log N_S: |S|=R+1\}
\]

be the packet-frequency subset-product frequency set, prime-supported and basis-normalized. Let \(J\) be an interval with

\[
N_{Z_\alpha}(J)\ge Q^{-C}|J|\rho_Z
\]

for the relevant zero density \(\rho_Z\). Suppose the zero-sampling kernel satisfies the operator estimate

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\left(
\frac{K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)}{K_J(0)}
\right)_{S,T}
-
I
\right\|_{\ell^2(\mathcal F)\to\ell^2(\mathcal F)}
\le
1-Q^{-C}.
}
\]

Then for every coefficient family \(\mathbf A_S\),

\[
\sum_{m_j\in\mathcal Z_J}w_J(m_j)
\left|
\sum_S\mathbf A_S e^{-im_j\Omega_S}
\right|^2
\ge
Q^{-C}K_J(0)\sum_S|\mathbf A_S|^2.
\]

In particular,

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{m_j\in\mathcal Z_J}w_J(m_j)|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J)
}
\]

after inserting the exterior coefficient mass and canonical error bounds.

This theorem is exactly the zero-set large sieve needed for \(|\mathbf M(m_0)|\) on average over zeros.

---

# 6. Argument-principle route

For an analytic surrogate \(H(s)\), one has formally

\[
\sum_{Z_\alpha(s_j)=0} H(s_j)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}
\int_{\partial\mathcal R}
H(s)\frac{Z_\alpha'(s)}{Z_\alpha(s)}\,ds,
\]

where \(\mathcal R\) contains the relevant zeros.

For the kernel,

\[
H(s)=w_J(s)e^{-is\xi},
\]

this gives

\[
K_J(\xi)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}
\int_{\partial\mathcal R}
w_J(s)e^{-is\xi}
\frac{Z_\alpha'(s)}{Z_\alpha(s)}\,ds.
\]

Thus kernel control reduces to estimating the Fourier transform of

\[
\frac{Z_\alpha'}{Z_\alpha}
\]

on the contour.

This is promising because logarithmic derivatives have explicit formula expansions. But several serious obstacles appear.

## Obstacle 1: poles near the contour

If zeros of \(Z_\alpha\) approach the contour, then

\[
Z_\alpha'/Z_\alpha
\]

is large. One needs a zero-avoiding contour or Cartan-type exceptional set.

## Obstacle 2: \(H=|\mathbf M|^2\) is not analytic

The desired sampling is

\[
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2.
\]

On the real line,

\[
|\mathbf M(m)|^2
=
\mathbf M(m)\overline{\mathbf M(m)}.
\]

An analytic replacement is

\[
\mathbf M(s)\mathbf M^\#(s),
\]

where

\[
\mathbf M^\#(s)=\overline{\mathbf M(\overline{s})}.
\]

This doubles the frequency set and must be kept under control.

## Obstacle 3: no obvious diagonal main term

The argument-principle expression gives a weighted sum involving \(Z_\alpha'/Z_\alpha\). To recover a positive diagonal term, one needs the zero distribution of \(Z_\alpha\) to behave like an equidistributed sampling measure for the subset-product phases.

That is already a deep zero-set large-sieve assertion.

So the argument principle gives a route to express \(K_J\), but not yet a proof.

---

# 7. Kernel via explicit formula

Using a logarithmic derivative expansion,

\[
\frac{Z_\alpha'}{Z_\alpha}(s)
\]

would produce prime-side terms. Then

\[
K_J(\xi)
\]

would become a sum of transformed prime frequencies shifted by \(\xi\).

The diagonal \(\xi=0\) gives zero count. For \(\xi\ne0\), one needs cancellation in

\[
\sum_{m_j}e^{-im_j\xi}.
\]

This is analogous to pair-correlation / spectral statistics of zeros, but for zeros of \(Z_\alpha\), not necessarily ordinary zeta zeros.

Therefore a sufficient condition is a pair-correlation-type bound:

\[
\boxed{
|K_J(\xi)|
\le
K_J(0)\,Q^{-C}
}
\]

on the relevant nonzero subset-product difference set, or the weaker operator version above.

This is a strong actual-zeta zero-statistics theorem.

---

# 8. Are resonances sparse enough?

For prime-supported subset products, exact resonances are absent except the diagonal. But near-resonances may be numerous.

Let

\[
\Omega_S-\Omega_T
=
\sum_{p} \varepsilon_p\log p,
\qquad
\varepsilon_p\in\{-1,0,1\}.
\]

This equals

\[
\log\frac{\prod_{\varepsilon_p=1}p}{\prod_{\varepsilon_p=-1}p}.
\]

The ratio is a rational number not equal to \(1\). The minimal possible size is at least roughly

\[
\left|\log\left(1+\frac1{N}\right)\right|\asymp N^{-1},
\]

where \(N\) can be as large as a subset product. Since

\[
\log N\asymp RQ,
\]

one gets a trivial lower bound

\[
|\Omega_S-\Omega_T|
\gtrsim e^{-RQ}.
\]

This is astronomically small, useless for interval diagonalization.

Thus near-resonance sparsity must be used statistically, not through a minimum gap.

---

# 9. No-go for a soft kernel proof

A soft proof would need to show that the matrix

\[
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)
\]

is automatically well-conditioned for the zero set. But this is false for arbitrary sampling sets: a set of points can concentrate on the small-value nodes of \(\mathbf M\), making the quadratic form small despite coefficient mass.

Therefore the zero-set nature of \(m_j\) must be used substantially. We need an actual theorem saying zeros of \(Z_\alpha\) are spectrally nonconcentrated relative to the subset-product frequency family.

This is not implied by:

\[
\sum_S|\mathbf A_S|^2\ge Q^{-C},
\]

nor by continuous \(L^2\) mass of \(\mathbf M\).

---

# 10. Final theorem candidate

The cleanest usable input is:

## Packet-frequency zero-set large sieve

Let \(Z_\alpha\) be the actual ordinary packet-frequency AFE object, and let

\[
\mathcal Z_J=\{m_j\in J:Z_\alpha(m_j)=0\}.
\]

Let

\[
\mathcal F_R
=
\{\Omega_S=\log N_S: S\subset\mathcal B_{\rm pf}, |S|=R+1\}.
\]

Then for every coefficient family \(B_S\),

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{m_j\in\mathcal Z_J}
w_J(m_j)
\left|
\sum_{S\in\mathcal F_R}
B_S e^{-im_j\Omega_S}
\right|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}
N_{Z_\alpha}(J)
\sum_S |B_S|^2.
}
\]

A vector-valued version follows by summing over components. Combined with exterior coefficient mass, this gives

\[
\sum_{m_j\in\mathcal Z_J}|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
\]

This would imply at least average non-smallness of \(\mathbf M\) on \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros. A pointwise statement would still need a local exclusion of rare bad zeros, or one can use it in a pigeonhole argument if the proof architecture allows selecting a good zero.

---

# 11. Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The zero-set large sieve is a precise and useful formulation, but it is a new actual-zeta zero-statistics theorem.}
}
\]

What is clear:

\[
\text{coefficient mass} \Rightarrow \text{continuous/average mass}.
\]

What is missing:

\[
\text{zeros of }Z_\alpha\text{ sample subset-product frequencies nondegenerately}.
\]

The kernel condition needed is:

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\left(
K_J(\Omega_S-\Omega_T)/K_J(0)
\right)_{S,T}
-
I
\right\|
\le
1-Q^{-C}.
}
\]

or an equivalent lower-frame inequality.

The argument principle gives a possible analytic representation of \(K_J\), but controlling it requires strong bounds on \(Z_\alpha'/Z_\alpha\) and zero correlations. Thus this lane reduces to an actual zero-set spectral large-sieve theorem, not a consequence of current Wronskian algebra.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 04:16:37)

_Thought for 6s_

## Agent 3 / C — Zero-crowding route with cell-sum amplitude

I am executing the **Agent 3 / C** instruction: connect the cell-sum non-cancellation theorem to Jensen/Cartan zero-crowding and the derivative lower bound for

\[
\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)
=
\left((-1)^\ell C_\ell(\mathcal D)Z_\alpha(m_0)\right)_{\ell=1}^{R+1}.
\]

The shared target is to prove

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

via polylog-local amplitude or cell-sum non-cancellation. 

---

# 1. Setup

Let

\[
F(t)=Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t),
\qquad
F(0)=0,
\qquad
\lambda Q\asymp1.
\]

On a packet-frequency block,

\[
F(t)
=
\sum_n b_n e^{-itx_n}
+
F_{\rm dual}(t)
+
F_{\rm bg}(t)
+
F_{\rm err}(t),
\]

where

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n,
\qquad
\operatorname{diam}\{x_n\}\asymp R^{-1},
\]

and

\[
b_n=z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}.
\]

Take a window

\[
a\in\{R,2R,(\log Q)^2\}.
\]

Partition the packet-frequency interval into cells

\[
I_r,
\qquad
|I_r|\asymp a^{-1}.
\]

Define

\[
B_r=\sum_{x_n\in I_r} b_n.
\]

The target cell-sum condition is

\[
\boxed{
\sum_r |B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

---

# 2. Cell sums imply local \(L^2\) amplitude

Let \(x_r\) be a representative point of \(I_r\). Then

\[
\sum_n b_ne^{-itx_n}
=
\sum_r e^{-itx_r}B_r
+
\mathcal E_{\rm cell}(t),
\]

where

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm cell}(t)
=
\sum_r
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
b_n\left(e^{-itx_n}-e^{-itx_r}\right).
\]

For \(|t|\le a\) and \(|x_n-x_r|\lesssim a^{-1}\),

\[
|e^{-itx_n}-e^{-itx_r}|
\lesssim |t||x_n-x_r|
\lesssim 1.
\]

This is not small automatically. To make the approximation stable, use either:

1. smaller cells

\[
|I_r|\le \eta a^{-1}
\]

with fixed small \(\eta\), or

2. define the cell basis by local averages

\[
B_r(t)=\sum_{x_n\in I_r}b_ne^{-it(x_n-x_r)}
\]

and use

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2dt
\]

directly.

The exact quadratic form is

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2dt
=
\sum_{r,s}
\sum_{\substack{x_n\in I_r\\x_{n'}\in I_s}}
b_n\overline{b_{n'}}
K_a(x_n-x_{n'}),
\]

where

\[
K_a(\xi)=2\frac{\sin(a\xi)}{\xi}.
\]

After cell aggregation, this becomes

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2dt
=
\sum_{r,s}B_r\overline{B_s}K_a(x_r-x_s)
+
\text{cell-error}
+
\text{dual/background/error}.
\]

If the cell Gram matrix

\[
G_{r,s}=K_a(x_r-x_s)
\]

has lower frame bound

\[
\sum_{r,s}B_r\overline{B_s}G_{r,s}
\ge c_a\sum_r|B_r|^2,
\]

with

\[
c_a\ge Q^{-C},
\]

and the cell-error plus dual/background/error terms are \(\le \frac12 Q^{-A}\), then

\[
\boxed{
\sum_r |B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}
\Longrightarrow
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2dt\ge Q^{-A'}.
}
\]

Then

\[
\sup_{|t|\le a}|F(t)|^2
\ge
\frac1{2a}\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2dt,
\]

so, since \(a\le(\log Q)^C\),

\[
\boxed{
\sup_{|t|\le a}|F(t)|\ge Q^{-A''}.
}
\]

Thus cell-sum non-cancellation gives polylog-local amplitude.

---

# 3. Annular amplitude from cell sums

The Jensen/Cartan route often wants amplitude away from the zero at \(t=0\), e.g.

\[
\sup_{a/2\le |t|\le a}|F(t)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Cell-sum \(L^2\) on \([-a,a]\) does not automatically force annular amplitude, because mass may concentrate near \(0\). But if

\[
F(0)=0
\]

and one has a derivative upper bound

\[
\sup_{|t|\le a}|F'(t)|\le Q^{C},
\]

then mass cannot concentrate on an interval of length \(Q^{-C'}\) only. More cleanly, use two windows:

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F|^2
-
\int_{-a/2}^{a/2}|F|^2.
\]

If the cell-sum lower bound holds at scale \(a\), while the smaller-window coherent part is controlled, then

\[
\boxed{
\sup_{a/2\le |t|\le a}|F(t)|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

A sufficient formulation is:

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2dt\ge Q^{-A_1},
\]

and

\[
\int_{-a/2}^{a/2}|F(t)|^2dt
\le \frac12 Q^{-A_1}.
\]

The second inequality is not automatic. It requires either:

- a multi-scale cell-sum condition showing mass appears only after resolving cells at scale \(a\), or
- direct annular cell-sum testing.

So the clean theorem should use annular \(L^2\):

\[
\boxed{
\int_{a/2\le |t|\le a}|F(t)|^2dt\ge Q^{-A}
\Rightarrow
\sup_{a/2\le |t|\le a}|F(t)|\ge Q^{-A'}.
}
\]

The cell-sum theorem should therefore be stated with the annular kernel

\[
K^{\rm ann}_a(\xi)
=
\int_{a/2\le |t|\le a}e^{-it\xi}\,dt.
\]

---

# 4. Best radius choice

## \(a=R\)

This is the first natural scale.

Since

\[
\operatorname{diam}x\asymp R^{-1},
\]

we have

\[
a\operatorname{diam}x\asymp1.
\]

So \(a=R\) barely resolves the packet. It is good for detecting one or a few low coarse modes. It may still miss higher packet anti-design.

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{
a=R\text{ is minimal and fragile.}
}
\]

## \(a=2R\)

This gives modestly better resolution:

\[
a\operatorname{diam}x\asymp2.
\]

The Gram matrix is less degenerate, but still low-bandwidth.

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{
a=2R\text{ is probably the best first theorem scale.}
}
\]

## \(a=(\log Q)^2\)

Since

\[
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\]

we get

\[
a\operatorname{diam}x
\asymp
(\log Q)^2\frac{\log\log Q}{\log Q}
=
(\log Q)(\log\log Q).
\]

This strongly resolves the packet. The cell width

\[
a^{-1}
\]

is much smaller than the packet width, producing many cells. The \(L^2\) lower bound becomes closer to a genuine large-sieve estimate.

Analytic loss remains acceptable because

\[
a^R
=
\exp(R\log a)
=
\exp\left(
\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q}\cdot 2\log\log Q
\right)
=
Q^2.
\]

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{
a=(\log Q)^2\text{ is the strongest admissible amplitude scale.}
}
\]

This is the preferred radius for the cell-sum route.

---

# 5. Zero-crowding once cell amplitude is available

Assume we have annular amplitude:

\[
\sup_{a/2\le |t|\le a}|F(t)|\ge Q^{-A_0}.
\]

Assume outer upper control:

\[
\sup_{|t|\le A}|F(t)|\le Q^{A_1},
\]

with

\[
A/a\ge(\log Q)^{C_*}.
\]

Then the amplitude-to-derivative theorem already gives

\[
\max_{1\le k\le R+1}\frac{|F^{(k)}(0)|}{k!}\ge Q^{-A}
\]

provided the first nonzero Taylor coefficient occurs before \(R+1\).

If multiplicity is unknown, zero-crowding supplies it. Specifically, if

\[
N_F(|t|\le A)\le C_0R,
\]

then

\[
\operatorname{ord}_0F\le C_0R.
\]

But we need

\[
\operatorname{ord}_0F\le R+1
\]

or at least \(\le C R\) with the derivative vector extended to \(CR\). Since the Wronskian route uses only \(R+1\) derivatives, the sufficient zero-crowding condition should be sharpened to

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{ord}_0F\le R+1.
}
\]

This does **not** follow from

\[
N_F(|t|\le A)\le C_0R
\]

unless \(C_0\le1\), which is too strong.

Therefore the cleanest package is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell-sum annular amplitude}
+
\operatorname{ord}_0F\le R+1
+
\text{outer upper control}
\Rightarrow
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Zero-crowding can replace the multiplicity bound only if it is strong enough to imply

\[
\operatorname{ord}_0F\le R+1.
\]

Otherwise it is a separate input.

---

# 6. Does cell-sum non-cancellation imply multiplicity bound?

No, not directly.

Cell-sum non-cancellation gives

\[
\sup_{|t|\le a}|F(t)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

But a function can have a high-order zero at \(0\) and still become large at radius \(a\). Example:

\[
F(t)=Q^{-A} \left(\frac{t}{a}\right)^M
\]

has

\[
\sup_{|t|\le a}|F(t)|=Q^{-A}
\]

but

\[
F^{(k)}(0)=0
\quad
(k<M).
\]

Thus amplitude alone does not bound the first \(R+1\) derivatives unless combined with outer upper control and a radius ratio that penalizes high multiplicity.

Indeed, Cauchy/Jensen gives

\[
Q^{-A_0}
\le
Q^{A_1}\left(\frac{a}{A}\right)^M
\]

if \(F\) has a zero of order \(M\) at \(0\). Therefore

\[
M
\le
\frac{A_0+A_1}{\log(A/a)/\log Q}.
\]

With

\[
A/a=(\log Q)^{C_*},
\]

we get

\[
M
\lesssim
\frac{(A_0+A_1)\log Q}{C_*\log\log Q}.
\]

So by choosing \(C_*\) large enough, we can force

\[
M\le R+1.
\]

Thus multiplicity can be controlled by amplitude plus outer upper control, provided the outer radius ratio is sufficiently large.

This recovers the amplitude-to-derivative theorem without a separate zero-crowding assumption.

---

# 7. Clean combined theorem

## Cell-sum to derivative theorem

Let

\[
F(t)=Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t),
\qquad F(0)=0,
\qquad R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q}.
\]

Let

\[
1\le a\le(\log Q)^{C_a},
\]

and suppose there is an outer radius

\[
A=a(\log Q)^{C_*}
\]

such that

\[
\sup_{|t|\le A}|F(t)|\le Q^{A_1}.
\]

Assume the packet-frequency AFE on \(|t|\le a\) admits a cell partition \(I_r\) with cell sums \(B_r\) satisfying the annular frame estimate

\[
\int_{a/2\le |t|\le a}|F(t)|^2dt
\ge
Q^{-C_1}\sum_r|B_r|^2
-
Q^{-C_2}.
\]

Assume the arithmetic cell-sum lower bound

\[
\sum_r|B_r|^2\ge Q^{-C_3}.
\]

Then

\[
\sup_{a/2\le |t|\le a}|F(t)|\ge Q^{-C_4}.
\]

If \(C_*\) is chosen sufficiently large in terms of \(A_1,C_4\), then

\[
\boxed{
\max_{1\le k\le R+1}\frac{|F^{(k)}(0)|}{k!}
\ge Q^{-C_5}.
}
\]

After converting monomial derivatives to basis-normalized operators,

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-C_6}.
}
\]

The conversion costs at most

\[
\exp(O(R\log R))=Q^{O(1)}.
\]

---

# 8. Is zero-crowding still needed?

Not if the cell-sum theorem gives annular amplitude and outer upper control is available with a large enough radius ratio.

Zero-crowding becomes an alternative route to annular amplitude or multiplicity control, not an essential separate input.

The clean dependency is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell-sum non-cancellation}
+
\text{outer analytic upper control}
\Rightarrow
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Zero-crowding is useful if cell-sum non-cancellation can only give a weak or non-annular amplitude statement.

---

# 9. Remaining arithmetic input

The only non-analytic part is now:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\sum_r|B_r(m_0)|^2\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is the actual cell-sum non-cancellation theorem.

In expanded form:

\[
B_r(m_0)
=
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}
+
\text{dual branch contribution in cell }r.
\]

So the theorem says the actual AFE phases cannot cancel simultaneously in all packet-frequency cells at a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero.

---

# 10. Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Cell-sum amplitude can bypass a separate zero-crowding theorem if it gives annular }L^2\text{ mass.}
}
\]

The preferred theorem package is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell-sum lower bound}
\Rightarrow
\text{annular local amplitude}
\Rightarrow
\text{multiplicity control via outer radius}
\Rightarrow
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The best radius is probably

\[
\boxed{
a=(\log Q)^2
}
\]

because it remains analytically safe while resolving the packet much better than \(a=R\).

The remaining unsolved arithmetic statement is the cell-sum lower bound:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\sum_r
\left|
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}
\right|^2
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is narrower than the full Wronskian incidence theorem and is now the best subclaim to attack for proving \(|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}\).

## Assistant (2026-04-27 05:43:47)

_Thought for 4s_

## Agent 3 / C — Cell-sum theorem versus zero-set large sieve

I am executing the **Agent 3 / C** instruction: compare the cell-sum theorem with the zero-set large-sieve theorem for \(\mathbf M\), and decide whether one can imply or support the other. The current prompt explicitly warns that these may be separate arithmetic inputs and asks for a common-kernel comparison. 

---

# 1. The two inputs

## A. Cell-sum theorem for local \(Z_\alpha\)-amplitude

The cell-sum theorem is a pointwise statement at a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\sum_r |B_r(m_0)|^2\ge Q^{-A},
\]

where

\[
B_r(m_0)
=
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n},
\]

or, in full main+dual form,

\[
B_r
=
B_r^+
+
e^{i\Theta_Z(m_0)}B_r^-
+
B_r^{\rm bg}
+
B_r^E.
\]

This is meant to imply polylog-local amplitude:

\[
\sup_{|t|\le a}|Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t)|\ge Q^{-A'},
\]

and therefore

\[
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A''}.
\]

The frequency family is the **linear packet-frequency family**

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n.
\]

---

## B. Zero-set large sieve for the minor vector

The zero-set large sieve is an averaged statement over many \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros:

\[
\sum_{\substack{m_j\in J\\Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
\]

The cofactor vector has expansion

\[
\mathbf M(m)
=
\sum_{|S|=R+1}
\mathbf A_S e^{-im\Omega_S}
+
\text{dual/background/error},
\]

where

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S,
\qquad
N_S=\prod_{n\in S}n.
\]

The frequency family is the **subset-product family**

\[
\Omega_S=\sum_{n\in S}\log n.
\]

Thus the two problems live on different frequency sets:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell sums: } \lambda\log n,
\qquad
\text{minor vector: } \log N_S.
}
\]

---

# 2. Common sampling-kernel framework

Both statements can be expressed as lower-frame inequalities for exponential sums sampled on \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros.

Let

\[
\mathcal Z_J=\{m_j\in J:Z_\alpha(m_j)=0\}.
\]

Define the zero-sampling kernel

\[
K_J(\xi)
=
\sum_{m_j\in \mathcal Z_J}
w_J(m_j)e^{-im_j\xi}.
\]

For a generic exponential sum

\[
H(m)=\sum_{\omega\in\Omega} a_\omega e^{-im\omega},
\]

sampling on \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros gives

\[
\sum_{m_j\in\mathcal Z_J}
w_J(m_j)|H(m_j)|^2
=
\sum_{\omega,\omega'}
a_\omega\overline{a_{\omega'}}
K_J(\omega-\omega').
\]

So both target the same abstract lower-frame problem:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{\omega,\omega'}
a_\omega\overline{a_{\omega'}}
K_J(\omega-\omega')
\ge
Q^{-A}K_J(0)\sum_\omega |a_\omega|^2.
}
\]

The difference is the choice of \(\Omega\):

\[
\Omega_{\rm cell}
=
\{\log n:n\in \text{packet-frequency cells}\},
\]

or after cell aggregation,

\[
\Omega_{\rm cell}^{\rm coarse}
=
\{\text{cell representatives}\},
\]

whereas

\[
\Omega_{\rm minor}
=
\{\log N_S: |S|=R+1\}.
\]

Thus there is a common large-sieve language, but not the same theorem.

---

# 3. Does the cell-sum theorem imply the zero-set large sieve for \(\mathbf M\)?

No, not directly.

The cell-sum theorem controls linear packet pieces of \(Z_\alpha\):

\[
B_r(m_0)
=
\sum_{x_n\in I_r} z_n e^{-im_0\log n}.
\]

It says the actual orbit cannot make all coarse packet cells small at a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero.

The minor vector, however, is built from exterior products of \(q''_{\rm can}\)-jet rows:

\[
\mathbf M(m)
=
\sum_{|S|=R+1}\mathbf A_S e^{-im\log N_S}.
\]

Even if every linear cell sum of \(Z_\alpha\) is large, the subset-product sum defining \(\mathbf M\) can still cancel:

\[
\sum_S \mathbf A_S e^{-im\log N_S}\approx0.
\]

The frequencies \(\log N_S\) are high-degree additive combinations of packet frequencies. A lower bound at degree \(1\) does not imply a lower bound at exterior degree \(R+1\).

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell-sum non-cancellation does not imply }|\mathbf M|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

It only supports the \(|\mathbf z'|\) lane.

---

# 4. Does zero-set large sieve for \(\mathbf M\) imply local \(Z_\alpha\)-amplitude?

Also no.

The zero-set large sieve controls values of \(\mathbf M\) on zeros of \(Z_\alpha\):

\[
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2.
\]

It says nothing about the local behavior of \(Z_\alpha\) near one of its zeros:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t).
\]

A zero could be locally flat, giving

\[
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ll Q^{-A},
\]

while \(\mathbf M(m_0)\) is large. Since

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0)
=
\langle \mathbf z'(m_0),\mathbf M(m_0)\rangle,
\]

large \(\mathbf M\) alone cannot compensate for a flat \(Z_\alpha\)-zero.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{zero-set large sieve for }\mathbf M
\text{ does not imply local }Z_\alpha\text{-amplitude.}
}
\]

It supports the \(|\mathbf M|\) lane, not the \(|\mathbf z'|\) lane.

---

# 5. A possible common theorem

There is, however, a common **zero-set spectral nonconcentration** theorem that would imply both if stated broadly enough.

Let \(\Omega\) be a frequency family generated from packet-frequency blocks. Suppose for every coefficient vector \(a_\omega\) in an admissible class,

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{m_j\in\mathcal Z_J}
w_J(m_j)
\left|
\sum_{\omega\in\Omega}a_\omega e^{-im_j\omega}
\right|^2
\ge
Q^{-A}K_J(0)
\sum_{\omega\in\Omega}|a_\omega|^2.
}
\]

If this holds for:

\[
\Omega=\Omega_{\rm cell}^{\rm coarse},
\]

then it gives zero-set nonconcentration for cell sums averaged over zeros.

If it holds for:

\[
\Omega=\Omega_{\rm minor},
\]

then it gives the minor-vector zero-set large sieve.

A very strong version covering all bounded-degree polynomial frequency combinations

\[
\omega=\sum_j \varepsilon_j\log n_j,
\qquad
|\varepsilon|_1\le R+1,
\]

would cover both.

But this is a much stronger actual-zeta zero-statistics theorem. It is not implied by either input separately.

---

# 6. Frequency-family comparison

## Cell frequencies

Cell-sum frequencies are linear:

\[
\omega_n=\log n.
\]

After normalization,

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n.
\]

They occupy packet-frequency intervals of width

\[
\Delta x\asymp R^{-1}
\]

or, in a multi-block setting, several such intervals.

Cell sums are coarse projections of a linear Dirichlet polynomial.

## Minor-vector frequencies

Minor-vector frequencies are subset products:

\[
\Omega_S=\log N_S
=
\sum_{n\in S}\log n,
\qquad
|S|=R+1.
\]

They occupy a much wider additive-combinatorial spectrum. Their differences are

\[
\Omega_S-\Omega_T
=
\log\frac{N_S}{N_T}.
\]

Even for prime-supported blocks, exact collisions are absent except \(S=T\), but near-resonances are potentially dense.

Thus the minor-vector large sieve is significantly harder.

\[
\boxed{
\Omega_{\rm minor}
\text{ is a high-degree additive-combinatorial lift of }
\Omega_{\rm cell}.
}
\]

---

# 7. Can cell-sum information help \(\mathbf M\) indirectly?

Only weakly.

A cell-sum theorem gives that, at a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero,

\[
\sum_r|B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This means the \(Z_\alpha\) packet does not collapse in every coarse frequency cell. It supports:

\[
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

The minor vector \(\mathbf M\), however, is built from \(q''_{\rm can}\), not \(Z_\alpha\), and from exterior products, not linear cell sums.

To connect them, one would need an additional theorem coupling the \(Z_\alpha\)-cell profile to the \(q''_{\rm can}\)-minor profile. That would be something like:

\[
\boxed{
\sum_r|B_r^{Z_\alpha}|^2\ge Q^{-A}
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|\mathbf M|\ge Q^{-A'}
}
\]

at \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros.

No such structural implication is visible. It would be another arithmetic coupling theorem between \(Z_\alpha\) and \(q''_{\rm can}\).

---

# 8. Can \(\mathbf M\)-large-sieve information help cell sums?

Again, only weakly.

If one proved

\[
\sum_{Z_\alpha(m_j)=0}|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J),
\]

then most zeros in \(J\) have non-small \(\mathbf M\). But the cell-sum theorem is pointwise at an arbitrary admissible zero \(m_0\), and concerns \(Z_\alpha\) local amplitude.

At best, one could use both in a selection argument:

- choose a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero where \(\mathbf M\) is large by the large sieve;
- choose a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero where cell sums are large by a separate average theorem;
- hope they coincide.

But Gate 1 requires the specific admissible off-line center, not merely some zero. So average information is not enough unless the proof architecture can select the zero.

---

# 9. Separation theorem

The two inputs should therefore be treated as independent.

## Theorem A: Cell-sum non-cancellation

For every admissible \(Z_\alpha\)-zero \(m_0\),

\[
\boxed{
\sum_r
\left|
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}
\right|^2
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Consequence:

\[
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A'}.
\]

## Theorem B: Zero-set large sieve for \(\mathbf M\)

For a zero set \(\mathcal Z_J\),

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{m_j\in\mathcal Z_J}
|\mathbf M(m_j)|^2
\ge Q^{-A}N_{Z_\alpha}(J).
}
\]

Consequence:

\[
\mathbf M
\]

is non-small at many \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros, or pointwise if strengthened.

Theorem A is a linear packet-frequency non-cancellation statement.  
Theorem B is a subset-product zero-sampling statement.

They are structurally related only by the common zero-set kernel framework, not by implication.

---

# 10. Recommendation

Prioritize the cell-sum theorem first.

Reason:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Cell-sum non-cancellation is narrower and supports }|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The zero-set large sieve for \(\mathbf M\) is broader and harder because of subset-product frequencies. It likely requires a stronger actual zero-statistics theorem.

So the proof stack should treat them as separate inputs:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Input 1: cell-sum non-cancellation for }Z_\alpha.
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Input 2: zero-set large sieve / nonrecurrence for }\mathbf M.
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Input 3: angle/incidence theorem.}
}
\]

The cell-sum theorem does not close the Wronskian route alone, but it is the smallest current arithmetic unlock.
